<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Arthur Sullivan</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">For other people named Arthur Sullivan, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Arthur_Sullivan_(disambiguation)" title="Arthur Sullivan (disambiguation)">Arthur Sullivan (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sir_Arthur_Seymour_Sullivan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="298" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Sir_Arthur_Seymour_Sullivan.jpg/220px-Sir_Arthur_Seymour_Sullivan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/Sir_Arthur_Seymour_Sullivan.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sir_Arthur_Seymour_Sullivan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arthur Sullivan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Sir Arthur Seymour Sullivan</b> <a href="/wiki/Royal_Victorian_Order" title="Royal Victorian Order">MVO</a> (13 May 1842 – 22 November 1900) was an English composer. He is best known for his series of 14 <a href="/wiki/Comic_opera" title="Comic opera">operatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Sullivan" title="Gilbert and Sullivan">collaborations</a> with the dramatist <a href="/wiki/W._S._Gilbert" title="W. S. Gilbert">W. S. Gilbert</a>, including <i><a href="/wiki/H.M.S._Pinafore" title="H.M.S. Pinafore">H.M.S. Pinafore</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pirates_of_Penzance" title="The Pirates of Penzance">The Pirates of Penzance</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mikado" title="The Mikado">The Mikado</a></i>. His works comprise 23 operas, 13 major orchestral works, eight choral works and <a href="/wiki/Oratorio" title="Oratorio">oratorios</a>, two ballets, <a href="/wiki/Incidental_music" title="Incidental music">incidental music</a> to several plays, and numerous hymns and other church pieces, songs, and piano and chamber pieces. The best known of his hymns and songs include "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Onward_Christian_Soldiers" title="Onward Christian Soldiers">Onward Christian Soldiers</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/The_Lost_Chord" title="The Lost Chord">The Lost Chord</a>".</p>
<p>The son of a military bandmaster, Sullivan composed his first anthem at age eight. He was selected as soloist in the boys' choir of the <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Royal" title="Chapel Royal">Chapel Royal</a>. In 1856, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy_of_Music" title="Royal Academy of Music">Royal Academy of Music</a> awarded the first <a href="/wiki/Mendelssohn_Scholarship" title="Mendelssohn Scholarship">Mendelssohn Scholarship</a> to the 14-year-old Sullivan, allowing him to study first at the Academy and then in Germany, at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn_College_of_Music_and_Theatre" title="Felix Mendelssohn College of Music and Theatre">Leipzig Conservatoire</a>. His graduation piece was a suite of incidental music to <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tempest_(Sullivan)" title="The Tempest (Sullivan)">The Tempest</a></i>. When it was performed in London in 1862, it was an immediate sensation. Sullivan began his composing career with a series of ambitious works, interspersed with hymns, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parlour_ballads" title="Parlour ballads">parlour ballads</a> and other light pieces. Among his best received early pieces were a ballet, <i><a href="/wiki/L%27%C3%8Ele_Enchant%C3%A9e" title="L'Île Enchantée">L'Île Enchantée</a></i> (1864), and his <i><a href="/wiki/Symphony_in_E_(Sullivan)" title="Symphony in E (Sullivan)">Irish Symphony</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cello_Concerto_(Sullivan)" title="Cello Concerto (Sullivan)">Cello Concerto</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overture_in_C_(In_Memoriam)" title="Overture in C (In Memoriam)">Overture in C (In Memoriam)</a></i> (all in 1866). From 1861 to 1872, he supplemented his income by working as a church organist and music teacher, and writing hymns and songs.</p>
<p>In 1866, Sullivan composed a one-act <a href="/wiki/Comic_opera" title="Comic opera">comic opera</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cox_and_Box" title="Cox and Box">Cox and Box</a></i>, which is still widely performed. His most successful orchestral work, the <i><a href="/wiki/Overture_di_Ballo" title="Overture di Ballo">Overture di Ballo</a></i>, premiered in 1870. Sullivan's talent and native charm earned him many friends in musical and social circles, including <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>'s son <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Duke_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Alfred, Duke of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Alfred, Duke of Edinburgh</a>. In 1871, Sullivan wrote his first opera with W. S. Gilbert, <i><a href="/wiki/Thespis_(opera)" title="Thespis (opera)">Thespis</a></i>. Sullivan then produced his <i><a href="/wiki/Festival_Te_Deum" title="Festival Te Deum">Festival Te Deum</a></i> (1872), an oratorio, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Light_of_the_World_(Sullivan)" title="The Light of the World (Sullivan)">The Light of the World</a></i> (1873), and incidental music for <a href="/wiki/West_End_theatre" title="West End theatre">West End</a> productions of several Shakespeare plays. He also had conducting and academic appointments. In 1875, producer <a href="/wiki/Richard_D%27Oyly_Carte" title="Richard D'Oyly Carte">Richard D'Oyly Carte</a> reunited Gilbert and Sullivan to create a one-act piece, <i><a href="/wiki/Trial_by_Jury" title="Trial by Jury">Trial by Jury</a></i>, which was a surprise hit. Their 1878 opera <i>H.M.S. Pinafore</i> became an international sensation, as did <i>The Pirates of Penzance</i> (1879) and <i><a href="/wiki/Patience_(opera)" title="Patience (opera)">Patience</a></i> (1881). Sullivan never married but had a long love affair with an American socialite, <a href="/wiki/Fanny_Ronalds" title="Fanny Ronalds">Fanny Ronalds</a>. After the death of his brother <a href="/wiki/Fred_Sullivan" title="Fred Sullivan">Fred</a> in 1877, Sullivan supported Fred's large family financially for the rest of his life, effectively adopting his nephew <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Sullivan" title="Herbert Sullivan">Bertie</a>.</p>
<p>Carte used his profits from the Gilbert and Sullivan partnership to build the <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Theatre" title="Savoy Theatre">Savoy Theatre</a> in 1881, and their joint works then became known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savoy_operas" title="Savoy operas">Savoy operas</a>. Later hits in the series were <i><a href="/wiki/Iolanthe" title="Iolanthe">Iolanthe</a></i> (1882), <i>The Mikado</i> (1885), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Yeomen_of_the_Guard" title="The Yeomen of the Guard">The Yeomen of the Guard</a></i> (1888) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gondoliers" title="The Gondoliers">The Gondoliers</a></i> (1889). Sullivan was <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knighted</a> for his contributions to music in 1883. His infrequent serious pieces during the 1880s included two cantatas, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Martyr_of_Antioch" title="The Martyr of Antioch">The Martyr of Antioch</a></i> (1880) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Golden_Legend_(cantata)" title="The Golden Legend (cantata)">The Golden Legend</a></i> (1886), his most popular choral work. Sullivan's only serious opera, <i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe_(opera)" title="Ivanhoe (opera)">Ivanhoe</a></i>, though initially successful in 1891, was little-heard after that. Gilbert broke from Sullivan and Carte in 1890, quarrelling over expenses at the Savoy. They reunited in the 1890s for two more operas, but those did not achieve the popularity of their earlier works. Sullivan continued to compose comic operas with other librettists and wrote a number of other major and minor works throughout the decade. He died at the age of 58, regarded as Britain's foremost composer. His comic opera style served as a model for the generations of musical theatre composers that followed, and his music is still frequently performed, recorded and <a href="/wiki/Pastiche" title="Pastiche">pastiched</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_career">Life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginnings">Beginnings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Beginnings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arthur-sullivan-circa-1855.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1084" data-file-width="800" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Arthur-sullivan-circa-1855.jpg/170px-Arthur-sullivan-circa-1855.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Arthur-sullivan-circa-1855.jpg/255px-Arthur-sullivan-circa-1855.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Arthur-sullivan-circa-1855.jpg/340px-Arthur-sullivan-circa-1855.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arthur-sullivan-circa-1855.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sullivan as a chorister of the <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Royal" title="Chapel Royal">Chapel Royal</a>, circa 1855</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sullivan was born in <a href="/wiki/Lambeth" title="Lambeth">Lambeth</a>, London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> His parents were Thomas Sullivan (1805–1866), a military bandmaster, clarinettist and music teacher born in Ireland and raised in <a href="/wiki/Chelsea,_London" title="Chelsea, London">Chelsea</a>, London, and Mary Clementina (<i>née</i> Coghlan, 1811–1882), English born, of Irish and Italian descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Thomas Sullivan was based from 1845 to 1857 at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Military_College,_Sandhurst" title="Royal Military College, Sandhurst">Royal Military College, Sandhurst</a>, where he was the bandmaster and taught music privately to supplement his income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ancestry_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ancestry-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Young Sullivan became proficient with many of the instruments in the band and had composed an <a href="/wiki/Anthem" title="Anthem">anthem</a>, "By the waters of Babylon", by the age of eight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> He later recalled:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I was intensely interested in all that the band did, and learned to play every wind instrument, with which I formed not merely a passing acquaintance, but a real, life-long, intimate friendship. I gradually learned the peculiarities of each ... what it could do and what it was unable to do. I learned in the best possible way how to write for an orchestra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young4_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-young4-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>While recognising the boy's obvious musical talent, his father knew the insecurity of a musical career and discouraged him from pursuing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> While studying at a private school in <a href="/wiki/Bayswater" title="Bayswater">Bayswater</a>, Sullivan, then aged 11, persuaded his parents and the headmaster to allow him to apply for membership in the choir of the <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Royal" title="Chapel Royal">Chapel Royal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Despite concerns that Sullivan at nearly 12 years of age was too old to give much service as a <a href="/wiki/Boy_soprano" title="Boy soprano">treble</a> before his voice broke, he was accepted and soon became a soloist and, by 1856, was promoted to "first boy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Even at this age, Sullivan's health was delicate, and he was easily fatigued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs.2C_pp._12.E2.80.9313_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs.2C_pp._12.E2.80.9313-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan flourished under the training of the Reverend <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Helmore" title="Thomas Helmore">Thomas Helmore</a>, master of the choristers, and began to compose anthems and songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs11_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs11-11">[11]</a></sup> Helmore encouraged the young Sullivan's composing talent and arranged for one of his pieces, "O Israel", to be published in 1855, Sullivan's first published work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Helmore also enlisted Sullivan's assistance in creating harmonisations for a volume of <i>The Hymnal Noted</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-musicaltimes_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-musicaltimes-13">[13]</a></sup> and arranged for Sullivan's compositions to be performed; one of the boy's anthems was given at the Chapel Royal in <a href="/wiki/St_James%27s_Palace" title="St James's Palace">St James's Palace</a> under the direction of <a href="/wiki/George_Thomas_Smart" title="George Thomas Smart">Sir George Smart</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs11_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs11-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mendelssohn_scholar">Mendelssohn scholar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mendelssohn scholar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1911" data-file-width="1269" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Young_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg/170px-Young_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Young_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg/255px-Young_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Young_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg/340px-Young_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sullivan at age 16, in his Academy of Music uniform</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1856, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy_of_Music" title="Royal Academy of Music">Royal Academy of Music</a> awarded the first <a href="/wiki/Mendelssohn_Scholarship" title="Mendelssohn Scholarship">Mendelssohn Scholarship</a> to the 14-year-old Sullivan, granting him a year's training at the academy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs.2C_pp._12.E2.80.9313_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs.2C_pp._12.E2.80.9313-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[n 1]</a></sup> His principal teacher there was <a href="/wiki/John_Goss_(composer)" title="John Goss (composer)">John Goss</a>, whose own teacher, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Attwood_(composer)" title="Thomas Attwood (composer)">Thomas Attwood</a>, had been a pupil of <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mack_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-mack-16">[15]</a></sup> He studied piano with the head of the academy, <a href="/wiki/William_Sterndale_Bennett" title="William Sterndale Bennett">William Sterndale Bennett</a>, and with <a href="/wiki/Arthur_O%27Leary_(composer)" title="Arthur O'Leary (composer)">Arthur O'Leary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> During this year at the Royal Academy, Sullivan continued to sing solos with the Chapel Royal, which provided a small amount of spending money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan's scholarship was extended to a second year, and in 1858 in what the biographer <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Jacobs" title="Arthur Jacobs">Arthur Jacobs</a> calls an "extraordinary gesture of confidence"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs17_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs17-19">[18]</a></sup> the scholarship committee extended his grant for a third year so that he could study in Germany, at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn_College_of_Music_and_Theatre" title="Felix Mendelssohn College of Music and Theatre">Leipzig Conservatoire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs17_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs17-19">[18]</a></sup> While there, Sullivan studied composition with <a href="/wiki/Julius_Rietz" title="Julius Rietz">Julius Rietz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Reinecke" title="Carl Reinecke">Carl Reinecke</a>, counterpoint with <a href="/wiki/Moritz_Hauptmann" title="Moritz Hauptmann">Moritz Hauptmann</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Richter" title="Ernst Richter">Ernst Richter</a> and the piano with <a href="/wiki/Louis_Plaidy" title="Louis Plaidy">Louis Plaidy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Moscheles" title="Ignaz Moscheles">Ignaz Moscheles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> He was trained in <a href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn" title="Felix Mendelssohn">Mendelssohn's</a> ideas and techniques but was also exposed to a variety of musical styles, including <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Schubert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Verdi" title="Giuseppe Verdi">Verdi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Bach</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Wagner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs24_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs24-21">[20]</a></sup> Visiting a <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogue</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[n 2]</a></sup> he was so struck by some of the cadences and progressions of the music that thirty years later he could recall them for use in his serious opera, <i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe_(opera)" title="Ivanhoe (opera)">Ivanhoe</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs24_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs24-21">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though the scholarship was extended to permit Sullivan to spend one year in Leipzig, he stayed for three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> There, he became friendly with the impresario <a href="/wiki/Carl_Rosa" title="Carl Rosa">Carl Rosa</a> and the violinist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Joachim" title="Joseph Joachim">Joseph Joachim</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> For his last year at Leipzig, his father scraped together the money for living expenses, and the conservatoire assisted by waiving its fees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs23_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs23-27">[25]</a></sup> Sullivan credited his Leipzig period with tremendous musical growth. His graduation piece, completed in 1861, was a set of <a href="/wiki/Incidental_music" title="Incidental music">incidental music</a> to <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tempest_(Sullivan)" title="The Tempest (Sullivan)">The Tempest</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs24_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs24-21">[20]</a></sup> Revised and expanded, it was performed at the <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">Crystal Palace</a> in 1862, a year after his return to London, and was an immediate sensation. He began building a reputation as England's most promising young composer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rising_composer">Rising composer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Rising composer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan-colleagues-1870s.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="200" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/64/Sullivan-colleagues-1870s.jpg" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan-colleagues-1870s.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Colleagues and collaborators: clockwise from top left, <a href="/wiki/George_Grove" title="George Grove">George Grove</a>, <a href="/wiki/F._C._Burnand" title="F. C. Burnand">F. C. Burnand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_D%27Oyly_Carte" title="Richard D'Oyly Carte">Richard D'Oyly Carte</a>, <a href="/wiki/W._S._Gilbert" title="W. S. Gilbert">W. S. Gilbert</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sullivan embarked on his composing career with a series of ambitious works, interspersed with hymns, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parlour_songs" title="Parlour songs">parlour songs</a> and other light pieces in a more commercial vein. His compositions were not enough to support him financially, and from 1861 to 1872 he supplemented his income by working as a church organist, which he enjoyed, and as a music teacher, sometimes at the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_School" title="Crystal Palace School">Crystal Palace School</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> which he hated and gave up as soon as he could.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[n 3]</a></sup> Sullivan had an early chance to compose several pieces for royalty in connection with the wedding of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_VII_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Edward VII of the United Kingdom">Prince of Wales</a> in 1863.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan's association with works for voice and orchestra began with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Masque_at_Kenilworth" title="The Masque at Kenilworth">The Masque at Kenilworth</a></i> (Birmingham Festival, 1864).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup> During a spell as organist at <a href="/wiki/Royal_Opera_House" title="Royal Opera House">Covent Garden</a>, he composed his first ballet, <i><a href="/wiki/L%27%C3%8Ele_Enchant%C3%A9e" title="L'Île Enchantée">L'Île Enchantée</a></i> (1864).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup> In 1866, he premiered his <i><a href="/wiki/Symphony_in_E_(Sullivan)" title="Symphony in E (Sullivan)">Irish Symphony</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Cello_Concerto_(Sullivan)" title="Cello Concerto (Sullivan)">Cello Concerto</a></i>, his only works in each such genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> In the same year, his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overture_in_C_(In_Memoriam)" title="Overture in C (In Memoriam)">Overture in C (In Memoriam)</a></i>, commemorating the recent death of his father, was a commission from the <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_and_Norwich_Festival" title="Norfolk and Norwich Festival">Norwich Festival</a>. During his lifetime, it achieved considerable popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup> In 1867, his overture <i>Marmion</i> was premiered by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Philharmonic_Society" title="Royal Philharmonic Society">Philharmonic Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grove_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-grove-31">[29]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> called it "another step in advance on the part of the only composer of any remarkable promise that just at present we can boast."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> In the autumn of 1867, Sullivan travelled with <a href="/wiki/George_Grove" title="George Grove">George Grove</a> to Vienna in search of neglected manuscript scores by Schubert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> They found and copied several and were particularly excited about their final discovery, the score to <i><a href="/wiki/Rosamunde" title="Rosamunde">Rosamunde</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[n 4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan's first attempt at opera, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sapphire_Necklace" title="The Sapphire Necklace">The Sapphire Necklace</a></i> (1863–64) to a libretto by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_Fothergill_Chorley" title="Henry Fothergill Chorley">Henry F. Chorley</a>, was not produced and is now lost, except for the overture and two songs from the work, which were separately published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup> His first surviving opera, <i><a href="/wiki/Cox_and_Box" title="Cox and Box">Cox and Box</a></i> (1866), was written for a private performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[39]</a></sup> It then received charity performances in London and Manchester, and was later produced at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallery_of_Illustration" title="Gallery of Illustration">Gallery of Illustration</a>, where it ran for an extraordinary 264 performances. <a href="/wiki/W._S._Gilbert" title="W. S. Gilbert">W. S. Gilbert</a>, writing in <i><a href="/wiki/Fun_(magazine)" title="Fun (magazine)">Fun</a></i> magazine, pronounced the score superior to <a href="/wiki/F._C._Burnand" title="F. C. Burnand">F. C. Burnand</a>'s libretto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[40]</a></sup> Sullivan and Burnand were soon commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_German_Reed" title="Thomas German Reed">Thomas German Reed</a> for a two-act opera, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Contrabandista" title="The Contrabandista">The Contrabandista</a></i> (1867; revised and expanded as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chieftain" title="The Chieftain">The Chieftain</a></i> in 1894), but it did not do as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup> Sullivan wrote a group of seven <a href="/wiki/Part_song" title="Part song">part songs</a> in 1868, the best-known of which is "<a href="/wiki/The_Long_Day_Closes_(song)" title="The Long Day Closes (song)">The Long Day Closes</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hymnssongs_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-hymnssongs-46">[42]</a></sup> Sullivan's last major work of the 1860s was a short <a href="/wiki/Oratorio" title="Oratorio">oratorio</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prodigal_Son_(Sullivan)" title="The Prodigal Son (Sullivan)">The Prodigal Son</a></i>, premiered in <a href="/wiki/Worcester_Cathedral" title="Worcester Cathedral">Worcester Cathedral</a> as part of the 1869 <a href="/wiki/Three_Choirs_Festival" title="Three Choirs Festival">Three Choirs Festival</a> to much praise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1870s.3B_first_collaborations_with_Gilbert">1870s; first collaborations with Gilbert</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1870s; first collaborations with Gilbert">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sorc-Pin-Trial.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2972" data-file-width="2372" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Sorc-Pin-Trial.jpg/220px-Sorc-Pin-Trial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Sorc-Pin-Trial.jpg/330px-Sorc-Pin-Trial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Sorc-Pin-Trial.jpg/440px-Sorc-Pin-Trial.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sorc-Pin-Trial.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Early poster showing scenes from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sorcerer" title="The Sorcerer">The Sorcerer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/H.M.S._Pinafore" title="H.M.S. Pinafore">H.M.S. Pinafore</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Trial_by_Jury" title="Trial by Jury">Trial by Jury</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sullivan's most enduring orchestral work,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes14_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes14-48">[44]</a></sup> the <i><a href="/wiki/Overture_di_Ballo" title="Overture di Ballo">Overture di Ballo</a></i>, was composed for the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Triennial_Music_Festival" title="Birmingham Triennial Music Festival">Birmingham Festival</a> in 1870.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[n 5]</a></sup> Also in 1870, Sullivan met Gilbert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[46]</a></sup> 1871 was a busy year for Sullivan. He published his only <a href="/wiki/Song_cycle" title="Song cycle">song cycle</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Window_(song_cycle)" title="The Window (song cycle)">The Window; or, The Songs of the Wrens</a></i>, to words by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_Tennyson,_1st_Baron_Tennyson" title="Alfred Tennyson, 1st Baron Tennyson">Tennyson</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup> and he wrote the first of a series of suites of incidental music for <a href="/wiki/West_End_theatre" title="West End theatre">West End</a> productions of Shakespeare plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[n 6]</a></sup> Still in 1871, Sullivan composed a dramatic <a href="/wiki/Cantata" title="Cantata">cantata</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/On_Shore_and_Sea" title="On Shore and Sea">On Shore and Sea</a></i>, for the opening of the London International Exhibition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[50]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymn</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Onward,_Christian_Soldiers" title="Onward, Christian Soldiers">Onward, Christian Soldiers</a></i>, with words by <a href="/wiki/Sabine_Baring-Gould" title="Sabine Baring-Gould">Sabine Baring-Gould</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hymnssongs_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-hymnssongs-46">[42]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salvation_Army" title="Salvation Army">Salvation Army</a> adopted the latter as its favoured <a href="/wiki/Processional_hymn" title="Processional hymn">processional</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[51]</a></sup> and it became Sullivan's most enduring hymn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hymnssongs_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-hymnssongs-46">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the end of 1871, the impresario <a href="/wiki/John_Hollingshead" title="John Hollingshead">John Hollingshead</a> commissioned Sullivan to work with W. S. Gilbert to create the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_burlesque" title="Victorian burlesque">burlesque</a>-style comic opera <i><a href="/wiki/Thespis_(opera)" title="Thespis (opera)">Thespis</a></i> for the <a href="/wiki/Gaiety_Theatre,_London" title="Gaiety Theatre, London">Gaiety Theatre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[n 7]</a></sup> Played as a Christmas entertainment, it ran through to Easter 1872, a good run for such a piece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[n 8]</a></sup> After <i>Thespis</i>, Gilbert and Sullivan went their separate ways<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[58]</a></sup> until they collaborated on three parlour ballads in late 1874 and early 1875.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan's large-scale works of the early 1870s were the <i><a href="/wiki/Festival_Te_Deum" title="Festival Te Deum">Festival Te Deum</a></i> (Crystal Palace, 1872);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs75_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs75-68">[60]</a></sup> and the oratorio, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Light_of_the_World_(Sullivan)" title="The Light of the World (Sullivan)">The Light of the World</a></i> (Birmingham Festival, 1873).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs75_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs75-68">[60]</a></sup> He provided suites of incidental music for productions of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Merry_Wives_of_Windsor" title="The Merry Wives of Windsor">The Merry Wives of Windsor</a></i> at the Gaiety in 1874<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[61]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_(play)" title="Henry VIII (play)">Henry VIII</a></i> at the Theatre Royal, Manchester in 1877.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[62]</a></sup> He continued to compose hymns throughout the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[n 9]</a></sup> In 1873, Sullivan contributed songs to Burnand's Christmas "drawing room extravaganza", <i>The Miller and His Man</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1875, the manager of the <a href="/wiki/Royalty_Theatre" title="Royalty Theatre">Royalty Theatre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_D%27Oyly_Carte" title="Richard D'Oyly Carte">Richard D'Oyly Carte</a>, needed a short piece to fill out a bill with Offenbach's <i><a href="/wiki/La_P%C3%A9richole" title="La Périchole">La Périchole</a></i>. Carte had conducted Sullivan's <i>Cox and Box</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[n 10]</a></sup> Remembering that Gilbert had suggested a libretto to him, Carte reunited <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Sullivan" title="Gilbert and Sullivan">Gilbert and Sullivan</a>, and the result was the one-act <a href="/wiki/Comic_opera" title="Comic opera">comic opera</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Trial_by_Jury" title="Trial by Jury">Trial by Jury</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[n 11]</a></sup> <i>Trial</i>, starring Sullivan's brother Fred as the Learned Judge, became a surprise hit, earning glowing praise from the critics and playing for 300 performances over its first few seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen30_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen30-80">[69]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Telegraph" title="Daily Telegraph">Daily Telegraph</a></i> commented that the piece illustrated the composer's "great capacity for dramatic writing of the lighter class",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen30_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allen30-80">[69]</a></sup> and other reviews emphasised the felicitous combination of Gilbert's words and Sullivan's music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[70]</a></sup> One wrote, "it seems, as in the great <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wagner" title="Wagner">Wagnerian</a> operas, as though poem and music had proceeded simultaneously from one and the same brain."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World226_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-World226-82">[71]</a></sup> Soon after the opening of <i>Trial</i>, Sullivan wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Zoo" title="The Zoo">The Zoo</a></i>, another one-act comic opera, with a libretto by <a href="/wiki/B._C._Stephenson" title="B. C. Stephenson">B. C. Stephenson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[72]</a></sup> But the latter work had only a few short runs, and for the next 15 years Sullivan's sole operatic collaborator was Gilbert; the two created an additional 12 operas together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[73]</a></sup></p>
<table align="right">
<tr>
<td>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>

</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Sullivan also turned out more than 80 popular songs and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parlour_ballads" title="Parlour ballads">parlour ballads</a>, most of them written before the end of the 1870s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[74]</a></sup> His first popular song was "Orpheus with his Lute" (1866), and a well-received <a href="/wiki/Part_song" title="Part song">part song</a> was "Oh! hush thee, my babie" (1867).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-musicaltimes_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-musicaltimes-13">[13]</a></sup> The best known of his songs is "<a href="/wiki/The_Lost_Chord" title="The Lost Chord">The Lost Chord</a>" (1877, lyrics by <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_Anne_Procter" title="Adelaide Anne Procter">Adelaide Anne Procter</a>), written in sorrow at the death of his brother Frederic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[75]</a></sup> The sheet music for his best received songs sold in large numbers and were an important part of his income; many of them were adapted as dance pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>In this decade, Sullivan's conducting appointments included the <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a> Choral Union concerts (1875–77) and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Aquarium" title="Royal Aquarium">Royal Aquarium</a>, London (1876).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[78]</a></sup> In addition to his appointment as Professor of Composition at the Royal Academy of Music, of which he was a Fellow, he was appointed as the first Principal of the National Training School for Music in 1876.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-wright-90">[79]</a></sup> He accepted the latter post reluctantly, fearing that discharging the duties thoroughly would leave too little time for composing. In this he was correct, as his successor <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Parry" title="Hubert Parry">Hubert Parry</a> also discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[80]</a></sup> Sullivan was not effective in the post, and he resigned in 1881.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[n 12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan's next collaboration with Gilbert, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sorcerer" title="The Sorcerer">The Sorcerer</a></i> (1877), ran for 178 performances,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[81]</a></sup> a success by the standards of the day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[82]</a></sup> but <i><a href="/wiki/H.M.S._Pinafore" title="H.M.S. Pinafore">H.M.S. Pinafore</a></i> (1878), which followed it, turned Gilbert and Sullivan into an international phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[83]</a></sup> The bright and cheerful music of <i>Pinafore</i> was composed during a time when Sullivan suffered from excruciating pain from a kidney stone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[85]</a></sup> <i>Pinafore</i> ran for 571 performances in London, the then-second-longest theatrical run in history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[86]</a></sup> and more than 150 unauthorised productions were quickly mounted in America alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[n 13]</a></sup> Among other favourable reviews, <i>The Times</i> noted that the opera was an early attempt at the establishment of a "national musical stage" ... free from risqué French "improprieties" and without the "aid" of Italian and German musical models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[89]</a></sup> <i>The Times</i> and several of the other papers agreed, however, that while the piece was entertaining, Sullivan was capable of higher art, and frivolous light opera would hold him back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[90]</a></sup> This criticism would follow Sullivan throughout his career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baily250_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baily250-104">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1879, Sullivan suggested to a reporter from <i>The New York Times</i> the secret of his success with Gilbert: "His ideas are as suggestive for music as they are quaint and laughable. His numbers ... always give me musical ideas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MrSullivan_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-MrSullivan-105">[92]</a></sup> <i>Pinafore</i> was followed by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pirates_of_Penzance" title="The Pirates of Penzance">The Pirates of Penzance</a></i> in 1879, another international success, which opened in New York and then ran in London for 363 performances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_1880s">Early 1880s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early 1880s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leeds-1886-iln-23_October_1886.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2584" data-file-width="2861" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/43/Leeds-1886-iln-23_October_1886.jpg/220px-Leeds-1886-iln-23_October_1886.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/43/Leeds-1886-iln-23_October_1886.jpg/330px-Leeds-1886-iln-23_October_1886.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/43/Leeds-1886-iln-23_October_1886.jpg/440px-Leeds-1886-iln-23_October_1886.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leeds-1886-iln-23_October_1886.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scenes from the premiere of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Golden_Legend_(cantata)" title="The Golden Legend (cantata)">The Golden Legend</a></i> at the <a href="/wiki/Leeds_Festival_(classical_music)" title="Leeds Festival (classical music)">Leeds Music Festival</a>, 1886</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1880, Sullivan was appointed director of the triennial <a href="/wiki/Leeds_Festival_(classical_music)" title="Leeds Festival (classical music)">Leeds Music Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[94]</a></sup> For his first festival he was commissioned to write a sacred choral work. He chose <a href="/wiki/Henry_Hart_Milman" title="Henry Hart Milman">Henry Hart Milman</a>'s 1822 dramatic poem based on the life and death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Margaret_the_Virgin" title="Saint Margaret the Virgin">Saint Margaret the Virgin</a> for its basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[95]</a></sup> Gilbert adapted the libretto for Sullivan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[96]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Martyr_of_Antioch" title="The Martyr of Antioch">The Martyr of Antioch</a></i> premiered in October 1880. Sullivan was not a showy conductor, and some thought him dull and old fashioned on the podium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[n 14]</a></sup> but his composition had an enthusiastic reception and was frequently revived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[99]</a></sup> A grateful Sullivan presented his collaborator with an engraved silver cup inscribed "W.S. Gilbert from his friend Arthur Sullivan."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[n 15]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the run of <i>The Pirates of Penzance</i>, Carte opened the next Gilbert and Sullivan piece, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patience_(operetta)" title="Patience (operetta)">Patience</a></i>, in April 1881 at London's <a href="/wiki/Opera_Comique" title="Opera Comique">Opera Comique</a>, where their past three operas had played. In October, <i>Patience</i> transferred to the new, larger, state-of-the-art <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Theatre" title="Savoy Theatre">Savoy Theatre</a>, built with the profits of the previous Gilbert and Sullivan works. The rest of the partnership's collaborations were produced at the Savoy, as a result of which they are widely known as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savoy_Opera" title="Savoy Opera">Savoy Operas</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[101]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Iolanthe" title="Iolanthe">Iolanthe</a></i> (1882), Gilbert and Sullivan's fourth hit in a row, was the first of the operas to premiere at the new theatre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[102]</a></sup> Sullivan, despite the financial security of writing for the Savoy, increasingly viewed his work with Gilbert as unimportant, beneath his skills, and also repetitious. After <i>Iolanthe</i>, Sullivan had not intended to write a new work with Gilbert, but he suffered a serious financial loss when his broker went bankrupt in November 1882. Therefore, he concluded that his financial needs required him to continue writing Savoy operas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[103]</a></sup> In February 1883, he and Gilbert signed a five-year agreement with Carte, requiring them to produce a new comic opera on six months' notice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 22 May 1883, Sullivan was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knight_bachelor" title="Knight bachelor">knighted</a> by <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> for his "services ... rendered to the promotion of the art of music" in Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[105]</a></sup> The musical establishment, and many critics, believed that this should put an end to his career as a composer of comic opera – that a musical knight should not stoop below oratorio or <a href="/wiki/Grand_opera" title="Grand opera">grand opera</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baily250_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baily250-104">[91]</a></sup> Having just signed the five-year agreement, Sullivan suddenly felt trapped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[106]</a></sup> In mid-December, he bade farewell to his sister-in-law Charlotte, Fred's widow, who emigrated with her young family to America. Sullivan's oldest nephew, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Sullivan" title="Herbert Sullivan">Herbert</a>, stayed behind in England as his uncle's ward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[107]</a></sup> The next opera, <i><a href="/wiki/Princess_Ida" title="Princess Ida">Princess Ida</a></i> (1884, the duo's only three-act, <a href="/wiki/Blank_verse" title="Blank verse">blank verse</a> work), had a noticeably shorter run than its four predecessors, although Sullivan's score was praised. With box office receipts lagging in March 1884, Carte gave the six months' notice, under the partnership contract, requiring a new opera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[108]</a></sup> Sullivan's close friend, the composer <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Clay" title="Frederic Clay">Frederic Clay</a>, had suffered a serious stroke in early December 1883 that effectively ended his career at the age of 45. Sullivan, reflecting on this, on his own longstanding kidney problems, and on his desire to devote himself to more serious music, replied to Carte, "[I]t is impossible for me to do another piece of the character of those already written by Gilbert and myself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[109]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mikado-Savoy-1885.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="434" data-file-width="600" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c4/Mikado-Savoy-1885.jpg/220px-Mikado-Savoy-1885.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c4/Mikado-Savoy-1885.jpg/330px-Mikado-Savoy-1885.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c4/Mikado-Savoy-1885.jpg/440px-Mikado-Savoy-1885.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mikado-Savoy-1885.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Programme for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mikado" title="The Mikado">The Mikado</a></i>, 1885</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gilbert had already started work on a new opera involving a plot in which people fell in love against their wills after taking a magic lozenge. The composer wrote, on 1 April 1884 that he had "come to the end of my tether" with the operas: "I have been continually keeping down the music in order that not one [syllable] should be lost. ... I should like to set a story of human interest &amp; probability where the humorous words would come in a humorous (not serious) situation, &amp; where, if the situation were a tender or dramatic one the words would be of similar character."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[110]</a></sup> In a lengthy exchange of correspondence, Sullivan pronounced Gilbert's plot sketch (particularly the "lozenge" element) unacceptably mechanical, and too similar in both its grotesque "elements of topsyturveydom" and in actual plot to their earlier work, especially <i>The Sorcerer</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[n 16]</a></sup> and repeatedly requested that Gilbert find a new subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[111]</a></sup> The impasse was finally resolved on 8 May when Gilbert proposed a plot that did not depend on any supernatural device. The result was Gilbert and Sullivan's most successful work, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mikado" title="The Mikado">The Mikado</a></i> (1885).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[112]</a></sup> The piece ran for 672 performances, which was the second-longest run for any work of musical theatre, and one of the longest runs of any theatre piece, up to that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[n 17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_1880s">Later 1880s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Later 1880s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan_by_Millais.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="665" data-file-width="500" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f9/Sullivan_by_Millais.jpg/170px-Sullivan_by_Millais.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f9/Sullivan_by_Millais.jpg/255px-Sullivan_by_Millais.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f9/Sullivan_by_Millais.jpg/340px-Sullivan_by_Millais.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan_by_Millais.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait by <a href="/wiki/John_Everett_Millais" title="John Everett Millais">Millais</a> (1888) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(United_Kingdom)" title="National Portrait Gallery (United Kingdom)">National Portrait Gallery</a>, London. It hangs next to <a href="/wiki/Frank_Holl" title="Frank Holl">Frank Holl</a>'s 1886 portrait of <a href="/wiki/W._S._Gilbert" title="W. S. Gilbert">Gilbert</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1886, Sullivan composed his second and last large-scale choral work of the decade. It was a cantata for the Leeds Festival, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Golden_Legend_(cantata)" title="The Golden Legend (cantata)">The Golden Legend</a></i>, based on <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wadsworth_Longfellow" title="Henry Wadsworth Longfellow">Longfellow</a>'s poem of the same name. Apart from the comic operas, this proved to be Sullivan's best received full-length work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[114]</a></sup> It was given hundreds of performances during his lifetime, and at one point he declared a moratorium on its presentation, fearing that it would become over-exposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[115]</a></sup> Only <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">Handel</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Messiah_(Handel)" title="Messiah (Handel)">Messiah</a></i> was performed more often in Britain in the 1880s and 90s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hulme_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-hulme-133">[116]</a></sup> It remained in the repertory until about the 1920s, but since then it has been seldom performed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[117]</a></sup> although it received its first professional recording in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hulme_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-hulme-133">[116]</a></sup> The musical scholar and conductor <a href="/wiki/David_Russell_Hulme" title="David Russell Hulme">David Russell Hulme</a> writes that the work influenced <a href="/wiki/Edward_Elgar" title="Edward Elgar">Elgar</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Walton" title="William Walton">Walton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[n 18]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Ruddigore" title="Ruddigore">Ruddigore</a></i> followed <i>The Mikado</i> at the Savoy in 1887. It was profitable, but its nine-month run was disappointing compared with most of the earlier Savoy operas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[118]</a></sup> For their next piece, Gilbert submitted another version of the magic lozenge plot; Sullivan immediately rejected it. Gilbert finally proposed a comparatively serious opera, to which Sullivan agreed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[119]</a></sup> Although it was not a grand opera, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Yeomen_of_the_Guard" title="The Yeomen of the Guard">The Yeomen of the Guard</a></i> (1888) provided him with the opportunity to compose his most ambitious stage work to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[120]</a></sup> As early as 1883, Sullivan had been under pressure from the musical establishment to write a grand opera. In 1885, he told an interviewer, ""The opera of the future is a compromise [among the French, German and Italian schools] – a sort of eclectic school, a selection of the merits of each one. I myself will make an attempt to produce a grand opera of this new school. ... Yes, it will be an historical work, and it is the dream of my life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfc_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfc-139">[121]</a></sup> After <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i> opened, Sullivan turned once again to Shakespeare, composing incidental music for <a href="/wiki/Henry_Irving" title="Henry Irving">Henry Irving</a>'s production of <i><a href="/wiki/Macbeth" title="Macbeth">Macbeth</a></i> (1888).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan wished to produce further serious works with Gilbert. He had collaborated with no other librettist since 1875. But Gilbert felt that the reaction to <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i> had "not been so convincing as to warrant us in assuming that the public want something more earnest still."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WriteTwoOperas_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-WriteTwoOperas-141">[123]</a></sup> He proposed instead that Sullivan should go ahead with his plan to write a grand opera, but should continue also to compose comic works for the Savoy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[n 19]</a></sup> Sullivan was not immediately persuaded. He replied, "I have lost the liking for writing comic opera, and entertain very grave doubts as to my power of doing it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[n 20]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Sullivan soon commissioned a grand opera libretto from <a href="/wiki/Julian_Sturgis" title="Julian Sturgis">Julian Sturgis</a> (who was recommended by Gilbert), while suggesting to Gilbert that he revive an old idea for an opera set in colourful <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[125]</a></sup> The comic opera was completed first: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gondoliers" title="The Gondoliers">The Gondoliers</a></i> (1889) was a piece described by <a href="/wiki/Gervase_Hughes" title="Gervase Hughes">Gervase Hughes</a> as a pinnacle of Sullivan's achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes.2C_p._24_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes.2C_p._24-146">[126]</a></sup> It was the last great Gilbert and Sullivan success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1890s">1890s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1890s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ivanhoe-programme-1891.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1328" data-file-width="800" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Ivanhoe-programme-1891.jpg/170px-Ivanhoe-programme-1891.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Ivanhoe-programme-1891.jpg/255px-Ivanhoe-programme-1891.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Ivanhoe-programme-1891.jpg/340px-Ivanhoe-programme-1891.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ivanhoe-programme-1891.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe_(opera)" title="Ivanhoe (opera)">Ivanhoe</a></i>, 1891</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The relationship between Gilbert and Sullivan suffered its most serious breach in April 1890, during the run of <i>The Gondoliers</i>, when Gilbert objected to Carte's financial accounts for the production, including a charge to the partnership for the cost of new carpeting for the Savoy Theatre lobby. Gilbert believed that this was a maintenance expense that should be charged to Carte alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[128]</a></sup> Carte was building a new theatre to present Sullivan's forthcoming grand opera, and Sullivan sided with Carte, going so far as to testify erroneously as to certain old debts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[129]</a></sup> Gilbert took legal action against Carte and Sullivan and vowed to write no more for the Savoy, and so the partnership came to an acrimonious end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[130]</a></sup> Sullivan wrote to Gilbert in September 1890 that he was "physically and mentally ill over this wretched business. I have not yet got over the shock of seeing our names coupled ... in hostile antagonism over a few miserable pounds".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan's only grand opera, <i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe_(opera)" title="Ivanhoe (opera)">Ivanhoe</a></i>, based on <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe" title="Ivanhoe">novel</a>, opened at Carte's new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_English_Opera_House" title="Royal English Opera House">Royal English Opera House</a> on 31 January 1891. Sullivan completed the score too late to meet Carte's planned production date, and costs mounted; Sullivan was required to pay Carte a contractual penalty of £3,000 for his delay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[133]</a></sup> The production lasted for 155 consecutive performances, an unprecedented run for a serious opera, and earned good notices for its music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GP_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-GP-154">[134]</a></sup> Afterwards, Carte was unable to fill the new opera house with other opera productions, however, and <i>Ivanhoe</i> was blamed for the failure of the opera house. The opera passed into obscurity after a touring revival in 1894–95.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GP_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-GP-154">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[135]</a></sup> The episode was, as the critic <a href="/wiki/Herman_Klein" title="Herman Klein">Herman Klein</a> observed, "the strangest comingling of success and failure ever chronicled in the history of British lyric enterprise!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[136]</a></sup> Sullivan did not seriously consider writing grand opera again. Later in 1891, Sullivan composed music for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_Tennyson" title="Alfred Tennyson">Tennyson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Foresters" title="The Foresters">The Foresters</a></i>, which ran well at Daly's Theatre in New York in 1892, but failed in London the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[n 21]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chieftain_poster_1894.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="262" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chieftain_poster_1894.jpg/170px-Chieftain_poster_1894.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chieftain_poster_1894.jpg/255px-Chieftain_poster_1894.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Chieftain_poster_1894.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chieftain_poster_1894.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Poster for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chieftain" title="The Chieftain">The Chieftain</a></i> (1894)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sullivan returned to comic opera, but because of the fracture with Gilbert, he and Carte sought other collaborators. Sullivan's next piece was <i><a href="/wiki/Haddon_Hall_(opera)" title="Haddon Hall (opera)">Haddon Hall</a></i> (1892), with a libretto by <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Grundy" title="Sydney Grundy">Sydney Grundy</a> based loosely on the historical elopement of <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Vernon" title="Dorothy Vernon">Dorothy Vernon</a> with John Manners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[141]</a></sup> Although still comic, the tone and style of the work was considerably more serious and romantic than most of the operas with Gilbert. It enjoyed a modest popularity, running for 204 performances, and earned critical praise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[142]</a></sup> In 1895, Sullivan once more provided incidental music for the Lyceum, this time for <a href="/wiki/J._Comyns_Carr" title="J. Comyns Carr">J. Comyns Carr</a>'s <i><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/10/Arthur_Sullivan_-_Incidental_music_to_King_Arthur.pdf" title="Arthur Sullivan - Incidental music to King Arthur.pdf">King Arthur</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>The partnership with Gilbert had been so profitable that Carte and his wife sought to reunite the author and composer, eventually succeeding with the help of <a href="/wiki/Chappell_%26_Co." title="Chappell &amp; Co.">Tom Chappell</a>, their music publisher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[144]</a></sup> Their next opera, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utopia_Limited" title="Utopia Limited">Utopia Limited</a></i> (1893), ran for 245 performances, barely covering the expenses of the lavish production,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[145]</a></sup> although it was the longest run at the Savoy in the 1890s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[146]</a></sup> Sullivan came to disapprove of the leading lady, <a href="/wiki/Nancy_McIntosh" title="Nancy McIntosh">Nancy McIntosh</a>, and refused to write another piece featuring her, while Gilbert insisted that she must appear in his next opera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[147]</a></sup> Instead, Sullivan teamed up again with his old partner, F. C. Burnand. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chieftain" title="The Chieftain">The Chieftain</a></i> (1894), a heavily-revised version of their earlier two-act opera, <i>The Contrabandista</i>, flopped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[148]</a></sup> Gilbert and Sullivan reunited one more time, after McIntosh announced her retirement from the stage, for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Grand_Duke" title="The Grand Duke">The Grand Duke</a></i> (1896). This also failed, and Sullivan never worked with Gilbert again, although their operas continued to be revived with success at the Savoy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1897, Sullivan's full-length ballet, <i><a href="/wiki/Victoria_and_Merrie_England" title="Victoria and Merrie England">Victoria and Merrie England</a></i>, opened at the <a href="/wiki/Alhambra_Theatre" title="Alhambra Theatre">Alhambra Theatre</a> to celebrate the Queen's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee" title="Diamond Jubilee">Diamond Jubilee</a>. The work's seven scenes celebrate English history and culture, with the Victorian period as the grand finale. Its six-month run was considered a great achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[150]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beauty_Stone" title="The Beauty Stone">The Beauty Stone</a></i> (1898), with a libretto by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wing_Pinero" title="Arthur Wing Pinero">Arthur Wing Pinero</a> and J. Comyns Carr was based on mediaeval <a href="/wiki/Morality_play" title="Morality play">morality plays</a>. The collaboration did not go particularly well: Sullivan wrote that Pinero and Comyns Carr were "gifted and brilliant men, with <i>no</i> experience in writing for music",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[151]</a></sup> and, when he asked for alterations to improve the structure, they refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[152]</a></sup> Sullivan's score, moreover, was too serious for the Savoy audiences' tastes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[153]</a></sup> The opera was a critical failure and did not attract a following, running for only seven weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1899, to benefit "the wives and children of soldiers and sailors" on active service in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_African_War" title="South African War">Boer War</a>, Sullivan composed the music of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jingoistic" title="Jingoistic">jingoistic</a> song, "<a href="/wiki/The_Absent-Minded_Beggar" title="The Absent-Minded Beggar">The Absent-Minded Beggar</a>", to a text by <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">Rudyard Kipling</a>, which became an instant sensation and raised an unprecedented £250,000 from performances and the sale of sheet music and related merchandise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[155]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rose_of_Persia" title="The Rose of Persia">The Rose of Persia</a></i> (1899), Sullivan returned to his comic roots, writing to a libretto by <a href="/wiki/Basil_Hood" title="Basil Hood">Basil Hood</a> that combined an exotic <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_Nights" title="Arabian Nights">Arabian Nights</a></i> setting with plot elements of <i>The Mikado</i>. Sullivan's tuneful score was well received, and the opera proved to be his most successful full-length collaboration apart from those with Gilbert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[156]</a></sup> Another opera with Hood, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Emerald_Isle" title="The Emerald Isle">The Emerald Isle</a></i>, quickly went into preparation, but Sullivan died before it could be completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death.2C_honours_and_legacy">Death, honours and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Death, honours and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_influence_of_Gilbert_and_Sullivan" title="Cultural influence of Gilbert and Sullivan">Cultural influence of Gilbert and Sullivan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_To_Sir_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="550" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Memorial_To_Sir_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg/200px-Memorial_To_Sir_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Memorial_To_Sir_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg/300px-Memorial_To_Sir_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Memorial_To_Sir_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg/400px-Memorial_To_Sir_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_To_Sir_Arthur_Sullivan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Memorial to Sullivan, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Embankment" title="Victoria Embankment">Victoria Embankment</a> Gardens, London</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Having suffered from long-standing recurrent kidney disease that made it necessary, from the 1880s, for him to conduct sitting down, Sullivan died of heart failure, following an attack of bronchitis, at his flat in London on 22 November 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-odnb_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-179">[158]</a></sup> His unfinished opera, <i>The Emerald Isle</i>, was completed by <a href="/wiki/Edward_German" title="Edward German">Edward German</a> and produced in 1901. His <i><a href="/wiki/Te_Deum_Laudamus_(Sullivan)" title="Te Deum Laudamus (Sullivan)">Te Deum Laudamus</a></i>, written to commemorate the end of the Boer War, was performed posthumously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>A monument in the composer's memory featuring a weeping <a href="/wiki/Muse" title="Muse">Muse</a> was erected in the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Embankment" title="Victoria Embankment">Victoria Embankment</a> Gardens in London and is inscribed with Gilbert's words from <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i>: "Is life a boon? If so, it must befall that Death, whene'er he call, must call too soon". Sullivan wished to be buried in <a href="/wiki/Brompton_Cemetery" title="Brompton Cemetery">Brompton Cemetery</a> with his parents and brother, but by order of the Queen he was buried in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St. Paul's Cathedral">St. Paul's Cathedral</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[160]</a></sup> In addition to his knighthood, honours awarded to Sullivan in his lifetime included Doctor in Music, <i>honoris causa</i>, by the universities of <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">Cambridge</a> (1876) and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford</a> (1879); Chevalier, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%C3%A9gion_d%27honneur" title="Légion d'honneur">Légion d'honneur</a>, France (1878); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medjidie" title="Medjidie">The Order of the Medjidieh</a> conferred by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Sultan of Turkey</a> (1888); and appointment as a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Victorian_Order" title="Royal Victorian Order">Member of the Fourth Class of the Royal Victorian Order</a> (MVO) in 1897.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-musicaltimes_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-musicaltimes-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>In all, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_musical_compositions_by_Arthur_Sullivan" title="List of musical compositions by Arthur Sullivan">Sullivan's artistic output</a> included 23 operas, 13 major orchestral works, eight choral works and oratorios, two ballets, one song cycle, incidental music to several plays, numerous hymns and other church pieces, and a large body of songs, parlour ballads, part songs, carols, and piano and chamber pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grove_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-grove-31">[29]</a></sup> Sullivan's operas have often been adapted, first in the 19th century as dance pieces<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[162]</a></sup> and in foreign adaptations of the operas themselves. Since then, his music has been made into ballets (<i><a href="/wiki/Pineapple_Poll" title="Pineapple Poll">Pineapple Poll</a></i> (1951) and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pirates_of_Penzance_-_The_Ballet!" title="Pirates of Penzance - The Ballet!">Pirates of Penzance - The Ballet!</a></i> (1991)) and musicals (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Swing_Mikado" title="The Swing Mikado">The Swing Mikado</a></i> (1938), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hot_Mikado_(1939_production)" title="The Hot Mikado (1939 production)">The Hot Mikado</a></i> (1939) and <i><a href="/wiki/Hot_Mikado" title="Hot Mikado">Hot Mikado</a></i> (1986), <i><a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Pinafore" title="Hollywood Pinafore">Hollywood Pinafore</a></i> (1945), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Black_Mikado" title="The Black Mikado">The Black Mikado</a></i> (1975), etc.). His operas are not only frequently performed, but also frequently <a href="/wiki/Parody" title="Parody">parodied</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pastiche" title="Pastiche">pastiched</a>, quoted and imitated in <a href="/wiki/Cultural_influence_of_Gilbert_and_Sullivan" title="Cultural influence of Gilbert and Sullivan">comedy routines, advertising, law, film, television, and other popular media</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PeterDowns_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-PeterDowns-184">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[164]</a></sup> His legacy, apart from writing the Savoy operas and his other works, is felt perhaps most strongly today through his influence on the American and British musical theatre. The innovations in content and form of the works that he and Gilbert developed directly influenced the development of the modern musical throughout the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PeterDowns_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-PeterDowns-184">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[165]</a></sup> In addition, biographies and scholarly articles and analyses continue to be written about Sullivan's life and work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Romantic_life">Romantic life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Romantic life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fanny_ronalds_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="372" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Fanny_ronalds_2.jpg/170px-Fanny_ronalds_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Fanny_ronalds_2.jpg/255px-Fanny_ronalds_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Fanny_ronalds_2.jpg/340px-Fanny_ronalds_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fanny_ronalds_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Fanny_Ronalds" title="Fanny Ronalds">Fanny Ronalds</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sullivan never married, but he had serious love affairs with several women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[n 22]</a></sup> The first was with Rachel Scott Russell (1845–1882), the daughter of the engineer <a href="/wiki/John_Scott_Russell" title="John Scott Russell">John Scott Russell</a>. Sullivan was a frequent visitor at the Scott Russell home in the mid-1860s, and by 1865 the affair was in full bloom.
