Rachel's parents did not approve of a possible union with a young composer with uncertain financial prospects, but the two continued to see each other covertly. At some point in 1868, Sullivan started a simultaneous (and secret) affair with Rachel's sister Louise (1841–1878). Both relationships had ceased by early 1869.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan's longest love affair was with the American socialite, <a href="/wiki/Fanny_Ronalds" title="Fanny Ronalds">Fanny Ronalds</a>, a woman three years his senior, who had two children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ainger128_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-ainger128-191">[169]</a></sup> He met her in Paris around 1867, and the affair began in earnest soon after she moved to London permanently in 1871.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ainger128_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-ainger128-191">[169]</a></sup> A contemporary account described Fanny Ronalds this way: "Her face was perfectly divine in its loveliness, her features small and exquisitely regular. Her hair was a dark shade of brown – <i>châtain foncé</i> [deep chestnut] – and very abundant ... a lovely woman, with the most generous smile one could possibly imagine, and the most beautiful teeth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[170]</a></sup> Sullivan called her "the best amateur singer in London".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[171]</a></sup> She often performed Sullivan's songs at her famous Sunday soirees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ainger128_191-2"><a href="#cite_note-ainger128-191">[169]</a></sup> She became particularly associated with "The Lost Chord", singing it both in private and in public, often with Sullivan accompanying her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[172]</a></sup> When Sullivan died, he left her the autograph manuscript of that song, along with other bequests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ronalds was separated from her American husband, but they never divorced. Social conventions of the time compelled Sullivan and Ronalds to keep their relationship private. In his diary, he would refer to her as "Mrs. Ronalds" when he saw her in a public setting, but "L. W." (for "Little Woman") or "D. H." (possibly "Dear Heart") when they were alone together, often with a number in parentheses indicating the number of sexual acts completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[175]</a></sup> Ronalds was apparently pregnant on at least two occasions and procured abortions in 1882 and 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[176]</a></sup> Sullivan had a roving eye, and his diary records the occasional quarrel when Ronalds discovered his other liaisons, but he always returned to her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[n 23]</a></sup> She was a constant companion up to the time of Sullivan's death, but around 1889 or 1890, the sexual relationship seems to have ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[178]</a></sup> He started to refer to her in the diary as "Auntie",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[179]</a></sup> and the tick marks indicating sexual activity were no longer there, although similar notation continued to be used for his relationships with other women who have not been identified, and who were always referred to by their initials.</p>
<p>In 1896, the 54-year-old Sullivan proposed marriage to the 22-year-old Violet Beddington (1874–1962), but she refused him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs-204">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leisure_and_family_life">Leisure and family life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Leisure and family life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bertie_and_arthur_sullivan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="611" data-file-width="400" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e5/Bertie_and_arthur_sullivan.jpg/170px-Bertie_and_arthur_sullivan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e5/Bertie_and_arthur_sullivan.jpg/255px-Bertie_and_arthur_sullivan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e5/Bertie_and_arthur_sullivan.jpg/340px-Bertie_and_arthur_sullivan.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bertie_and_arthur_sullivan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sullivan and his nephew <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Sullivan" title="Herbert Sullivan">Herbert ("Bertie")</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sullivan loved to spend time in France (both in Paris and the south of France), where his acquaintances included European royalty and where the casinos enabled him to indulge his passion for gambling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[182]</a></sup> He enjoyed hosting private dinners and entertainments at his home, often featuring famous singers and well-known actors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GGReminiscence_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-GGReminiscence-206">[183]</a></sup> In 1865 he was initiated into <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasonry</a> and was Grand Organist of the <a href="/wiki/United_Grand_Lodge_of_England" title="United Grand Lodge of England">United Grand Lodge of England</a> in 1887 during Queen Victoria's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Jubilee#For_Queen_Victoria" title="Golden Jubilee">Golden Jubilee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[184]</a></sup> Sullivan's talent and native charm gained him the friendship of many not only in the musical establishment, such as Grove, Chorley, and Herman Klein, but also in society circles, such as <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Duke_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Alfred, Duke of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Alfred, Duke of Edinburgh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[185]</a></sup> Sullivan enjoyed playing tennis although, according to <a href="/wiki/George_Grossmith" title="George Grossmith">George Grossmith</a>, "I have seen some bad lawn-tennis players in my time, but I never saw anyone so bad as Arthur Sullivan".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GGReminiscence_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-GGReminiscence-206">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan was devoted to his parents, particularly his mother, with whom he corresponded regularly, when away from London, until her death in 1882. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Lytton" title="Henry Lytton">Henry Lytton</a> wrote, "I believe there was never a more affectionate tie than that which existed between [Sullivan] and his mother, a very witty old lady, and one who took an exceptional pride in her son's accomplishments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[186]</a></sup> Sullivan was also very fond of his brother Fred, whose acting career he assisted whenever possible,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[n 24]</a></sup> and of Fred's children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[191]</a></sup> When Fred died at the age of 39, he left his pregnant wife, Charlotte, with seven children under the age of 14. After Fred's death, Arthur visited the family often and became guardian to all of the children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late 1883, Charlotte and six of her children emigrated to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Angeles,_California" title="Los Angeles, California">Los Angeles, California</a>, leaving the oldest boy, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Sullivan" title="Herbert Sullivan">"Bertie"</a>, in Sullivan's sole care. Despite his reservations about the move to America, Sullivan paid all the costs and gave substantial financial support to the family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[193]</a></sup> Only a year later, in January 1885, Charlotte died, leaving the six children to be raised mostly by her brother and the older girls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[194]</a></sup> From June to August 1885, after <i>The Mikado</i> premiered, Sullivan visited the family in Los Angeles and took them on a sightseeing trip of the American west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[195]</a></sup> Throughout the rest of his life, and in his will, he contributed financially to Fred's children, continuing to correspond with them and to be concerned with their education, marriages and financial affairs. Bertie stayed with his uncle Arthur for the rest of the composer's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>Three of Sullivan's cousins, the daughters of his uncle John Thomas Sullivan, performed with D'Oyly Carte: Rose, Jane ("Jennie") and Kate Sullivan, the first two of whom used the stage surname Hervey. Kate was a chorister who defected to the Comedy Opera Company's rival production of <i>H.M.S. Pinafore</i>, where she had the opportunity to play Josephine in 1879.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[197]</a></sup> Jennie was a D'Oyly Carte chorister for fourteen years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[198]</a></sup> Rose took principal roles in many of the <a href="/wiki/Savoy_opera#Companion_pieces" title="Savoy opera">companion pieces</a> that played with the Savoy operas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[200]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_musical_compositions_by_Arthur_Sullivan" title="List of musical compositions by Arthur Sullivan">List of musical compositions by Arthur Sullivan</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Method_of_composition_and_text_setting">Method of composition and text setting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Method of composition and text setting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arthur_sullivan_by_ape.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1232" data-file-width="796" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Arthur_sullivan_by_ape.jpg/170px-Arthur_sullivan_by_ape.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Arthur_sullivan_by_ape.jpg/255px-Arthur_sullivan_by_ape.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Arthur_sullivan_by_ape.jpg/340px-Arthur_sullivan_by_ape.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arthur_sullivan_by_ape.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sullivan by the cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Pellegrini_(caricaturist)" title="Carlo Pellegrini (caricaturist)">"Ape"</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sullivan told an interviewer, Arthur Lawrence, "I don't use the piano in composition – that would limit me terribly". Sullivan explained that his process was not to wait for inspiration, but "to dig for it. ... I decide on [the rhythm] before I come to the question of melody. ... I mark out the metre in dots and dashes, and not until I have quite settled on the rhythm do I proceed to actual notation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrence2_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrence2-225">[201]</a></sup> Sullivan's text setting, compared with that of his 19th century English predecessors or his European contemporaries, was "vastly more sensitive. ... Sullivan's operatic style attempts to create for itself a uniquely English text-music synthesis", and, in addition, by adopting a conservative musical style, he was able to achieve "the clarity to match Gilbert's finely honed wit with musical wit of his own."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>In composing the Savoy operas, Sullivan wrote the vocal lines of the musical numbers first, and these were given to the actors. He, or an assistant, improvised a piano accompaniment at the early rehearsals; he wrote the orchestrations later, after he had seen what Gilbert's stage business would be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrence2_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrence2-225">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[203]</a></sup> He left the overtures until last and often delegated their composition, based on his outlines, to his assistants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[204]</a></sup> often adding his suggestions or corrections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes130_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes130-229">[205]</a></sup> Those Sullivan wrote himself include <i>Thespis</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[206]</a></sup> <i>Iolanthe</i>, <i>Princess Ida</i>, <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i>, <i>The Gondoliers</i>, <i>The Grand Duke</i> and probably <i>Utopia Limited</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[207]</a></sup> Most of the overtures are structured as a <a href="/wiki/Potpourri_(music)" title="Potpourri (music)"><i>potpourri</i></a> of tunes from the operas in three sections: fast, slow and fast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes130_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes130-229">[205]</a></sup> Those for <i>Iolanthe</i> and <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i> are written in a modified <a href="/wiki/Sonata_form" title="Sonata form">sonata form</a>. The overtures from the Gilbert and Sullivan operas remain popular, and there are many recordings of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[208]</a></sup> Sullivan invariably conducted the operas on their opening nights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1957, a review in <i>The Times</i> gave this rationale for "the continued vitality of the Savoy operas":</p>
<dl>
<dd>"[T]hey were never really contemporary in their idiom. ... Gilbert and Sullivan's [world was] an artificial world, with a neatly controlled and shapely precision. ... For this, each partner has his share of credit. The neat articulation of incredibilities in Gilbert's plots is perfectly matched by his language. ... [Of] equal importance ... Gilbert's lyrics almost invariably take on extra point and sparkle when set to Sullivan's music. ... Sullivan's tunes, in these operas, also exist in a make-believe world of their own. ... [He is] a delicate wit, whose airs have a precision, a neatness, a grace, and a flowing melody".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[210]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Melody_and_rhythm">Melody and rhythm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Melody and rhythm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>The Musical Times</i> noted that Sullivan's tunes, at least in the comic operas, appeal to the professional as much as to the layman: his continental contemporaries such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint-Sa%C3%ABns" title="Saint-Saëns">Saint-Saëns</a> and the Viennese critic <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Hanslick" title="Eduard Hanslick">Eduard Hanslick</a> held the Savoy operas in high regard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[211]</a></sup> Hughes writes, "When Sullivan wrote what we call 'a good tune' it was nearly always 'good music' as well. Outside the ranks of the giants there are few other composers of whom the same could be said."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes129_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes129-236">[212]</a></sup> Although his melodies sprang from rhythm,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrence2_225-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrence2-225">[201]</a></sup> some of his themes may have been prompted by his chosen instrumentation or his harmonic techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes129_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes129-236">[212]</a></sup></p>
<table align="left">
<tr>
<td>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:1914_-_Edison_Light_Opera_Company_-_Favorite_airs_from_The_Mikado_(restored).ogg" title="File:1914 - Edison Light Opera Company - Favorite airs from The Mikado (restored).ogg">"Favorite airs from <i>The Mikado</i>"</a></div>
<div>
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="255.12925170068" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="1914_-_Edison_Light_Opera_Company_-_Favorite_airs_from_The_Mikado_(restored).ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_1" preload="none" style="width:220px">
<source data-bandwidth="118859" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (119 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e8/1914_-_Edison_Light_Opera_Company_-_Favorite_airs_from_The_Mikado_%28restored%29.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'></source></audio></div>
</div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">A 1914 <a href="/wiki/Edison_Records" title="Edison Records">Edison Records</a> recording of selections from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mikado" title="The Mikado">The Mikado</a></i>. Includes parts of the overture, "A wand'ring minstrel", "Three little maids", "Tit-willow", and the Act II finale.</div>
</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In the comic operas, where many numbers were in verse-plus-refrain form, Sullivan frequently produced two climaxes in the melodic line. Hughes instances "If you go in" (<i>Iolanthe</i>) as a good example. In Hughes's view, though most of the tunes in the Savoy operas are good ones, Sullivan rarely reached the same class of excellence elsewhere when he had no librettist to feed his imagination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[213]</a></sup> Even so, on those occasions when Gilbert wrote in unvaried metre, Sullivan often followed suit and produced phrases of simple repetition, such as "Love is a plaintive song" (<i>Patience</i>) and "A man who would woo a fair maid" (<i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[214]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan's deliberate echoes of other composers are covered below under "Musical Quotations", but other echoes may not have been conscious: Hughes cites the concluding bars of "Tell a tale of cock and bull" from <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i> as an example of Handel's influence, and another critic, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Greenfield" title="Edward Greenfield">Edward Greenfield</a>, found a theme in the slow movement of the <i>Irish Symphony</i> "an outrageous crib" from Schubert's <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._8_(Schubert)" title="Symphony No. 8 (Schubert)"><i>Unfinished</i> Symphony</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes.2C_p._152_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes.2C_p._152-239">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[216]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harmony_and_counterpoint">Harmony and counterpoint</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Harmony and counterpoint">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YoG-ms.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1596" data-file-width="1642" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d0/YoG-ms.jpg/220px-YoG-ms.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d0/YoG-ms.jpg/330px-YoG-ms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d0/YoG-ms.jpg/440px-YoG-ms.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:YoG-ms.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sullivan's manuscript for Act 1 finale, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Yeomen_of_the_Guard" title="The Yeomen of the Guard">The Yeomen of the Guard</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>Harmony</dt>
</dl>
<p>Sullivan was trained in the classical style, and contemporary music did not greatly attract him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes44_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes44-241">[217]</a></sup> Harmonically his early works used the conventional formulae of <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Auber" title="Daniel Auber">Auber</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donizetti" title="Donizetti">Donizetti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_William_Balfe" title="Michael William Balfe">Balfe</a> and Schubert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes44_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes44-241">[217]</a></sup> Later he drew on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gounod" title="Gounod">Gounod</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bizet" title="Bizet">Bizet</a>. Mendelssohn's influence, conspicuous in early works, appears intermittently in later ones. As a contemporary writer observed, Sullivan draws on these various influences while remaining recognisably himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[218]</a></sup> Yet, in the field of harmony, Hughes writes, Sullivan remained an eclectic: "He had easily recognisable <i>habits</i> but his style never achieved individuality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes44_241-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes44-241">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>In general, Sullivan preferred to write in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_key" title="Major key">major keys</a>. In the Savoy operas, there are only eleven substantial numbers wholly in a minor key (less than 5% of the musical numbers), and even in his serious works the major prevails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes52_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes52-243">[219]</a></sup> Examples of Sullivan's rare excursions into minor keys include the long E minor melody in the first movement of the <i>Irish Symphony</i>, "Go away, madam" in the Act I finale of <i>Iolanthe</i> (echoing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verdi" title="Verdi">Verdi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beethoven" title="Beethoven">Beethoven</a>) and the funeral march in the Act I finale of <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes52_243-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes52-243">[219]</a></sup> Sullivan was happy on occasion to use chords traditionally considered technically incorrect. When reproached for using consecutive <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">fifths</a> in <i>Cox and Box</i>, he replied "if 5ths turn up it doesn't matter, so long as there is no offence to the ear."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-musicaltimes_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-musicaltimes-13">[13]</a></sup> Hughes comments that harmonic contrast in the Savoy works is enhanced by Sullivan's characteristic modulation between keys, as in "Expressive glances" (<i>Princess Ida</i>), where he smoothly negotiates E major, C sharp minor and C major, or "Then one of us will be a queen" (<i>The Gondoliers</i>), where he writes in F major, D flat major and D minor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[220]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both Hughes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[221]</a></sup> and Jacobs in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grove_Dictionary_of_Music_and_Musicians" title="Grove Dictionary of Music and Musicians">Grove's Dictionary of Music and Musicians</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grove_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-grove-31">[29]</a></sup> comment adversely on Sullivan's over-use of <a href="/wiki/Pedal_point" title="Pedal point">tonic pedals</a>, usually in the bass, which Hughes attributes to "lack of enterprise or even downright laziness". Another Sullivan trademark criticised by Hughes is the repeated use of the chord of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augmented_fourth" title="Augmented fourth">augmented fourth</a> at moments of pathos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[222]</a></sup> In his serious works, Sullivan attempted to avoid harmonic devices associated with the Savoy operas, with the result, according to Hughes, that <i>The Golden Legend</i> is a "hotch-potch of harmonic styles".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[223]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Counterpoint</dt>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Sullivan,_conductor,_by_Lyall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="518" data-file-width="960" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fe/Arthur_Sullivan%2C_conductor%2C_by_Lyall.jpg/300px-Arthur_Sullivan%2C_conductor%2C_by_Lyall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fe/Arthur_Sullivan%2C_conductor%2C_by_Lyall.jpg/450px-Arthur_Sullivan%2C_conductor%2C_by_Lyall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fe/Arthur_Sullivan%2C_conductor%2C_by_Lyall.jpg/600px-Arthur_Sullivan%2C_conductor%2C_by_Lyall.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Sullivan,_conductor,_by_Lyall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sullivan as a conductor, c. 1879</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite his thorough contrapuntal training in London and Leipzig, as well as his experience as a church organist, Sullivan rarely composed fugues. Hughes cites examples from the Epilogue to <i>The Golden Legend</i> and <i>Victoria and Merrie England</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[224]</a></sup> In the Savoy operas, fugal style is reserved for making fun of legal solemnity in <i>Trial by Jury</i> and <i>Iolanthe</i> (e.g., the Lord Chancellor's leitmotif in the latter).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h75_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-h75-249">[225]</a></sup> Less formal counterpoint is employed in numbers such as "Brightly Dawns our Wedding Day" (<i>The Mikado</i>) and "When the Buds are Blossoming" (<i>Ruddigore</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h75_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-h75-249">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan's best known contrapuntal device was "the simultaneous presentation of two or more distinct melodies previously heard independently".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h78_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-h78-250">[226]</a></sup> He was not the first composer to combine themes in this way, but it became a characteristic feature of the Gilbert and Sullivan operas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h78_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-h78-250">[226]</a></sup> Sometimes the melodies were for solo voices, as in "Once more the face I loved so well" (<i>The Zoo</i>), and "I am so proud" (<i>The Mikado</i>), which combines three melodic lines;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[227]</a></sup> other examples are in choruses, where typically a graceful tune for the ladies is combined with a robust one for the men. Examples include "When the Foeman bares his steel" (<i>The Pirates of Penzance</i>), "In a doleful train" (<i>Patience</i>) and "Welcome, gentry" (<i>Ruddigore</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[228]</a></sup> At other times, notably in "How beautifully blue the sky" (<i>The Pirates of Penzance</i>), one theme is given to the chorus and the other to solo voices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[229]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orchestration">Orchestration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Orchestration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gervase Hughes concludes his chapter on Sullivan's orchestration: "[I]n this vitally important sector of the composer's art he deserves to rank as a master."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[230]</a></sup> Sullivan was a competent player of at least four orchestral instruments (flute, clarinet, trumpet and trombone) and a technically highly skilled orchestrator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[n 25]</a></sup> Though sometimes inclined to indulge in grandiosity when writing for a full symphony orchestra, he was adept in using smaller forces to the maximum effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[231]</a></sup> Percy Young writes that orchestral players generally like playing Sullivan's music: "Sullivan never asked his players to do what was either uncongenial or impracticable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[233]</a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Overture_di_ballo.ogg" title="File:Overture di ballo.ogg">Overture di Ballo</a></div>
<div>
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="677.40734693878" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Overture_di_ballo.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_2" preload="none" style="width:220px">
<source data-bandwidth="136628" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (137 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/81/Overture_di_ballo.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'></source></audio></div>
</div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">The <i><a href="/wiki/Overture_di_Ballo" title="Overture di Ballo">Overture di Ballo</a></i> (1870) is regarded as Sullivan's most successful orchestral work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes14_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes14-48">[44]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Military_band" title="Military band">military band</a> arrangement is performed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Band" title="United States Marine Band">U.S. Marine Band</a>.</div>
</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Sullivan's orchestra for the Savoy Operas was typical of any other pit orchestra of his era: 2 flutes (+ <a href="/wiki/Piccolo" title="Piccolo">piccolo</a>), oboe, 2 clarinets, <a href="/wiki/Bassoon" title="Bassoon">bassoon</a>, 2 <a href="/wiki/French_horn" title="French horn">horns</a>, 2 <a href="/wiki/Cornet" title="Cornet">cornets</a>, 2 trombones, <a href="/wiki/Timpani" title="Timpani">timpani</a>, percussion and strings. According to <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Toye" title="Geoffrey Toye">Geoffrey Toye</a>, the number of players in Sullivan's Savoy Theatre orchestras was a "minimum" of 31.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[234]</a></sup> Sullivan argued hard for an increase in the pit orchestra's size, and starting with <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i>, the orchestra was augmented with a second bassoon and a second tenor trombone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[235]</a></sup> Sullivan generally orchestrated each score at almost the last moment, noting that the accompaniment for an opera had to wait until he saw the staging, so that he could judge how heavily or lightly to orchestrate each part of the music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[236]</a></sup> For his large-scale orchestral pieces, Sullivan added a second oboe part, sometimes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_bassoon" title="Double bassoon">double bassoon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bass_clarinet" title="Bass clarinet">bass clarinet</a>, more horns, trumpets, tuba, and sometimes an organ and/or a harp. Many of these pieces used very large orchestras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the most recognisable features in Sullivan's orchestration is his woodwind scoring. Hughes especially notes Sullivan's clarinet writing, exploiting all registers and colours of the instrument, and his particular fondness for oboe solos. For instance, the <i>Irish Symphony</i> contains two long solo oboe passages in succession, and in the Savoy operas there are many shorter examples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[238]</a></sup> In the operas, and also in concert works, another characteristic Sullivan touch is his fondness for <a href="/wiki/Pizzicato" title="Pizzicato">pizzicato</a> passages for all the string sections. Most of the operas have at least one number that Hughes calls "virtually a <i>pizzicato <a href="/wiki/Ostinato" title="Ostinato">ostinato</a></i>"; he instances "Kind sir, you cannot have the heart" (<i>The Gondoliers</i>), "Free from his fetters grim" (<i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i>) and "In vain to us you plead" (<i>Iolanthe</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[239]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_quotations_and_parodies">Musical quotations and parodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Musical quotations and parodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan-1870.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="717" data-file-width="447" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sullivan-1870.jpg/170px-Sullivan-1870.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sullivan-1870.jpg/255px-Sullivan-1870.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sullivan-1870.jpg/340px-Sullivan-1870.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan-1870.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sullivan in about 1870</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sullivan often quoted or imitated famous themes and passages from well-known tunes or parodied the styles of famous composers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[240]</a></sup> He also liked to evoke familiar musical styles, such as his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madrigal_(music)" title="Madrigal (music)">madrigals</a> in <i>The Mikado</i>, <i>Ruddigore</i> and <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i>, <a href="/wiki/Glee_(music)" title="Glee (music)">glees</a> in <i>H.M.S. Pinafore</i> and <i>The Mikado</i> and <a href="/wiki/Gavotte" title="Gavotte">gavottes</a> in <i>Ruddigore</i> and <i>The Gondoliers</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h144_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-h144-266">[241]</a></sup> In <i>The Sorcerer</i>, there is a country dance in "If you'll marry me".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h144_266-1"><a href="#cite_note-h144-266">[241]</a></sup> In <i>Ruddigore</i>, the sailor character dances a hornpipe, while in <i>The Mikado</i>, Sullivan quotes a Japanese war song in "Miya Sama". Sullivan's 1882 trip to Egypt inspired musical styles in his later opera, <i>The Rose of Persia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[242]</a></sup> Of the sextette "I hear the soft note" in <i>Patience</i>, Sullivan said to the singers, "I think you will like this. It is <a href="/wiki/Michael_Arne" title="Michael Arne">Dr. Arne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Purcell" title="Henry Purcell">Purcell</a> at their best."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PallM_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-PallM-268">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early pieces, he imitated Mendelssohn (for example in his incidental music for <i>The Tempest</i>), Auber in his <i>Henry VIII</i> music and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gounod" title="Charles Gounod">Gounod</a> in <i>The Light of the World</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[244]</a></sup> In his comic operas, Sullivan followed <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Offenbach" title="Jacques Offenbach">Offenbach</a>'s lead in parodying the idioms of French and Italian opera, such as those of <a href="/wiki/Gaetano_Donizetti" title="Gaetano Donizetti">Donizetti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Bellini" title="Vincenzo Bellini">Bellini</a> and Verdi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[245]</a></sup> His music also shows the influence of Handel, Schubert and, conspicuously in the fairy music in <i>Iolanthe</i>, Mendelssohn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[246]</a></sup> The then-popular composer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Balfe" title="Michael Balfe">Michael Balfe</a> is parodied in <i>The Sorcerer</i> and <i>The Pirates of Penzance</i>, and "Twenty Love Sick Maidens" imitates <a href="/wiki/William_Vincent_Wallace" title="William Vincent Wallace">William Vincent Wallace</a>'s "Alas Those Chimes" from <i><a href="/wiki/Maritana" title="Maritana">Maritana</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[247]</a></sup> The sextet "A Nice Dilemma" in <i>Trial by Jury</i> parodies "D'un pensiero" in Bellini's <i><a href="/wiki/La_sonnambula" title="La sonnambula">La sonnambula</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scherer_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scherer-273">[248]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan_to_MacKenzie.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2888" data-file-width="2103" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Sullivan_to_MacKenzie.png/200px-Sullivan_to_MacKenzie.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Sullivan_to_MacKenzie.png/300px-Sullivan_to_MacKenzie.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Sullivan_to_MacKenzie.png/400px-Sullivan_to_MacKenzie.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan_to_MacKenzie.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sullivan depicted in 1897 teasing the leading academic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_MacKenzie_(composer)" title="Alexander MacKenzie (composer)">Alexander MacKenzie</a>, whose operetta, <i><a href="/wiki/His_Majesty_(comic_opera)" title="His Majesty (comic opera)">His Majesty</a></i>, failed at the <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Theatre" title="Savoy Theatre">Savoy</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Other examples of opera parody include Mabel's aria "Poor Wand'ring One" in <i>The Pirates of Penzance</i> and the duet "Who are you, sir?" from <i>Cox and Box</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[249]</a></sup> In <i>H.M.S. Pinafore</i>, the whispered plans for elopement in "This very night" parody the conspirators' choruses in Verdi's <i><a href="/wiki/Il_trovatore" title="Il trovatore">Il trovatore</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rigoletto" title="Rigoletto">Rigoletto</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scherer_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scherer-273">[248]</a></sup> and the octet, "Farewell, my own," evokes the ensemble "Mag der Himmel euch vergeben" in <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_von_Flotow" title="Friedrich von Flotow">Flotow's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Martha_(opera)" title="Martha (opera)">Martha</a></i> and such concerted numbers as the sextet in Donizetti's <i><a href="/wiki/Lucia_di_Lammermoor" title="Lucia di Lammermoor">Lucia di Lammermoor</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scherer_273-2"><a href="#cite_note-Scherer-273">[248]</a></sup> The mock-jingoistic "He is an Englishman" in <i>H.M.S. Pinafore</i> and choral passages in <i>The Zoo</i> satirise patriotic British tunes such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Arne" title="Thomas Arne">Arne's</a> "<a href="/wiki/Rule,_Britannia!" title="Rule, Britannia!">Rule, Britannia!</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scherer_273-3"><a href="#cite_note-Scherer-273">[248]</a></sup> The chorus "With catlike tread" from <i>Pirates</i> is an imitation of Verdi's "<a href="/wiki/Anvil_Chorus" title="Anvil Chorus">Anvil Chorus</a>" from <i>Il trovatore</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[250]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <i>Princess Ida</i>, there is a strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Frederick_Handel" title="George Frederick Handel">Handelian</a> flavour to Arac's song in Act III ("This helmet, I suppose"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes.2C_p._152_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes.2C_p._152-239">[215]</a></sup> and the Act II quartet "The world is but a broken toy" has been called "Gounodesque".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[251]</a></sup> Florian's statement in "Gently, Gently": "In this college, useful knowledge/Everywhere one finds" is a quotation from Chopin's Waltz No. 5 in A-flat Major (Op. 42).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[252]</a></sup> In <i>The Gondoliers</i>, there are the Spanish <a href="/wiki/Cachucha" title="Cachucha">cachucha</a>, the Italian <a href="/wiki/Saltarello" title="Saltarello">saltarello</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tarantella" title="Tarantella">tarantella</a>, and the Venetian <a href="/wiki/Barcarolle" title="Barcarolle">barcarolle</a>. Hughes compares "Here is a case unprecedented" from <i>The Gondoliers</i> to the Act II quintet from <a href="/wiki/Georges_Bizet" title="Georges Bizet">Bizet</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Carmen" title="Carmen">Carmen</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[253]</a></sup> In "A more humane Mikado", when the Mikado mentions "Bach interwoven with <a href="/wiki/Louis_Spohr" title="Louis Spohr">Spohr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Beethoven</a>", the clarinet and bassoon quote the fugue subject of Bach's <a href="/wiki/Great_Fantasia_and_Fugue_in_G_minor,_BWV_542" title="Great Fantasia and Fugue in G minor, BWV 542"><i>Fantasia and Fugue in G minor</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[254]</a></sup> <i>The Golden Legend</i> shows the influence of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Liszt</a> and Wagner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan frequently gives groups or locations their own characters and motifs. Hughes points to the striking difference between the male chorus of rustics in <i>The Sorcerer</i> and the eponymous gondoliers, and between the fairies in <i>Iolanthe</i> and the undergraduates in <i>Princess Ida</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h143_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-h143-281">[256]</a></sup> <i>H.M.S Pinafore</i> retains "a nautical tang <i>throughout</i>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h143_281-1"><a href="#cite_note-h143-281">[256]</a></sup> and in <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i> the Tower of London is evoked continually by its own motif.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h143_281-2"><a href="#cite_note-h143-281">[256]</a></sup> A distinctive four-note theme is associated with the character Iolanthe, and the Fairy Queen's music parodies that of Wagnerian heroines such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Br%C3%BCnnhilde" title="Brünnhilde">Brünnhilde</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[257]</a></sup> This use of Wagnerian leitmotif technique is repeated and developed further in <i>Ivanhoe</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[258]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reputation_and_criticism">Reputation and criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Reputation and criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Critical_reputation_of_Arthur_Sullivan" title="Critical reputation of Arthur Sullivan">Critical reputation of Arthur Sullivan</a></div>
<table cellspacing="5" class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; font-size: 85%; margin-right: 1em; background:#c6dbf7; color:black; width:30em; max-width: 50%;">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: center;">
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Punch_-_Sullivan%27s_knighthood.png"><img alt="Punch - Sullivan's knighthood.png" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="1224" height="424" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Punch_-_Sullivan%27s_knighthood.png/300px-Punch_-_Sullivan%27s_knighthood.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Punch_-_Sullivan%27s_knighthood.png/450px-Punch_-_Sullivan%27s_knighthood.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Punch_-_Sullivan%27s_knighthood.png/600px-Punch_-_Sullivan%27s_knighthood.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Punch_-_Sullivan%27s_knighthood.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;"><i>Cartoon from</i> <a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a> <i>in 1880. It was premature in declaring Sullivan's knighthood, but was accompanied by a <a href="/wiki/Parody" title="Parody">parody</a> version of "When I, good friends" from</i> <a href="/wiki/Trial_by_Jury" title="Trial by Jury">Trial by Jury</a> <i>that summarised Sullivan's career to that date:</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> </td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: center;">"A HUMOROUS KNIGHT."</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: center;">["It is reported that after the Leeds Festival Dr. Sullivan will be knighted." Having read this in a column of gossip, a be-nighted Contributor, who has "the Judge's Song" on the brain, suggests the following verse, adapted to probabilities.]</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">
<dl>
<dd>As a boy I had such a musical bump,</dd>
<dd>    And its size so struck Mr. H<small>ELMORE</small>,</dd>
<dd>That he said, "Though you sing those songs like a trump,</dd>
<dd>    You shall write some yourself that will sell more."</dd>
<dd>So I packed off to Leipsic, without <i>looking back,</i></dd>
<dd>    And returned in such classical fury,</dd>
<dd>That I sat down with H<small>ANDEL</small> and H<small>AYDN</small> and B<small>ACH</small>,—</dd>
<dd>    And turned out "<i>Trial by Jury."</i></dd>
<dd>    </dd>
<dd>But W.S.G. he jumped for joy</dd>
<dd>    As he said, "Though the job dismay you,</dd>
<dd>Send Exeter Hall to the deuce, my boy;</dd>
<dd>    It's the <i>haul</i> with me that'll pay you."</dd>
<dd>And we hauled so well, mid jeers and taunts,</dd>
<dd>    That we've settled, spite all temptations,</dd>
<dd>To stick to our Sisters and our Cousins and our Aunts,—</dd>
<dd>    And continue our pleasant relations.</dd>
<dd>    </dd>
<dd>Yet I know a big Duke, and I've written for Leeds,</dd>
<dd>    And I think (I don't wish to be snarly),</dd>
<dd>If honour's poured out on a chap for his deeds,</dd>
<dd>    I'm as good-—come, as M<small>ONCKTON</small> or C<small>HARLEY</small>!</dd>
<dd>So the next "first night" and the Opéra C.,</dd>
<dd>    Let's hope, if you're able to find him,</dd>
<dd>You'll cry from the pit, "There's W. S. G.</dd>
<dd>    In the stalls,—<i>with a</i> K<small>NIGHT</small> <i>behind him!'"</i></dd>
</dl>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_reception">Early reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Early reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sullivan's critical reputation has undergone extreme changes since he first came to prominence in the 1860s. At first, critics were struck by his potential, and he was hailed as the long-awaited great English composer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[259]</a></sup> His incidental music to <i>The Tempest</i> received an acclaimed premiere at the Crystal Palace just before Sullivan's 20th birthday in April 1862. The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athenaeum_(British_magazine)" title="Athenaeum (British magazine)">Athenaeum</a></i> wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It ... may mark an epoch in English music, or we shall be greatly disappointed. Years on years have elapsed since we have heard a work by so young an artist so full of promise, so full of fancy, showing so much conscientiousness, so much skill, and so few references to any model elect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[260]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>His <i>Irish Symphony</i> of 1866 won similarly enthusiastic praise, but as Arthur Jacobs notes, "The first rapturous outburst of enthusiasm for Sullivan as an orchestral composer did not last."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[261]</a></sup> A comment typical of those that followed him throughout his career was that "Sullivan's unquestionable talent should make him doubly careful not to mistake popular applause for artistic appreciation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[262]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Sullivan turned to comic opera with Gilbert, the serious critics began to express disapproval. Peter Gammond writes of "misapprehensions and prejudices, delivered to our door by the Victorian firm Musical Snobs Ltd. ... frivolity and high spirits were sincerely seen as elements that could not be exhibited by anyone who was to be admitted to the sanctified society of Art."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[263]</a></sup> As early as 1877 <i>The Figaro</i> wrote that Sullivan "has all the ability to make him a great composer, but he wilfully throws his opportunity away. ... He possesses all the natural ability to have given us an English opera, and, instead, he affords us a little more-or-less excellent fooling."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[264]</a></sup> Few critics denied the excellence of Sullivan's theatre scores. <i>The Theatre</i> wrote that "<i>Iolanthe</i> sustains Dr Sullivan's reputation as the most spontaneous, fertile, and scholarly composer of comic opera this country has ever produced."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[265]</a></sup> However, comic opera, no matter how skilfully crafted, was viewed as an intrinsically lower form of art than oratorio. <i>The Athenaeum's</i> review of <i>The Martyr of Antioch</i> declared: "[I]t is an advantage to have the composer of <i>H.M.S. Pinafore</i> occupying himself with a worthier form of art."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[266]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Knighthood_and_later_years">Knighthood and later years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Knighthood and later years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sullivan's knighthood in 1883 gave the serious music critics further ammunition. <i>The Musical Review</i> of that year wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[S]ome things that Mr. Arthur Sullivan may do, Sir Arthur ought not to do. In other words, it will look rather more than odd to see announced in the papers that a new comic opera is in preparation, the book by Mr. W. S. Gilbert and the music by Sir Arthur Sullivan. A musical knight can hardly write shop ballads either; he must not dare to soil his hands with anything less than an anthem or a madrigal; oratorio, in which he has so conspicuously shone, and symphony, must now be his line. Here is not only an opportunity, but a positive obligation for him to return to the sphere from which he has too long descended [and] do battle for the honour of English art ... against all foreign rivals, and arouse us thoroughly from our present half-torpid condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baily250_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baily250-104">[91]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Even Sullivan's friend George Grove wrote: "Surely the time has come when so able and experienced a master of voice, orchestra, and stage effect – master, too, of so much genuine sentiment – may apply his gifts to a serious opera on some subject of abiding human or natural interest."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baily250_104-3"><a href="#cite_note-Baily250-104">[91]</a></sup> Sullivan finally redeemed himself in critical eyes with <i>The Golden Legend</i> in 1886. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> hailed it as a "triumph of English art".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[267]</a></sup> <i>The World</i> called it "one of the greatest creations we have had for many years. Original, bold, inspired, grand in conception, in execution, in treatment, it is a composition which will make an "epoch" and which will carry the name of its composer higher on the wings of fame and glory. ... The effect of the public performance was unprecedented."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[268]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hopes for a new departure were evident in the <i>Daily Telegraph'</i>s review of <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i> (1888), Sullivan's most serious opera to that point: "[T]he music follows the book to a higher plane, and we have a genuine English opera, forerunner of many others, let us hope, and possibly significant of an advance towards a national lyric stage."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[269]</a></sup> Sullivan's only wholly serious opera, <i>Ivanhoe</i> (1891), received generally favourable reviews, although <a href="/wiki/John_Alexander_Fuller_Maitland" title="John Alexander Fuller Maitland">J. A. Fuller Maitland</a>, in <i>The Times</i>, expressed reservations, writing that the opera's "best portions rise so far above anything else that Sir Arthur Sullivan has given to the world, and have such force and dignity, that it is not difficult to forget the drawbacks which may be found in the want of interest in much of the choral writing, and the brevity of the concerted solo parts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[270]</a></sup> Sullivan's 1897 ballet <i><a href="/wiki/Victoria_and_Merrie_England" title="Victoria and Merrie England">Victoria and Merrie England</a></i> was one of several late pieces that won praise from most critics:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Sir Arthur Sullivan's music is music for the people. There is no attempt made to force on the public the dullness of academic experience. The melodies are all as fresh as last year's wine, and as exhilarating as sparkling champagne. There is not one tune which tires the hearing, and in the matter of orchestration our only humorist has let himself run riot, not being handicapped with libretto, and the gain is enormous. ... All through we have orchestration of infinite delicacy, tunes of alarming simplicity, but never a tinge of vulgarity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[271]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Although the more solemn members of the musical establishment could not forgive Sullivan for writing music that was both comic and accessible, he was, nevertheless, "the nation's <i>de facto</i> composer laureate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[n 26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Posthumous_reputation">Posthumous reputation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Posthumous reputation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the decade after his death, Sullivan's reputation sank considerably. In 1901, Fuller Maitland took issue with the generally laudatory tone of most of the obituaries: "Is there anywhere a case quite parallel to that of Sir Arthur Sullivan, who began his career with a work which at once stamped him as a genius, and to the height of which he only rarely attained throughout life? ... It is because such great natural gifts – gifts greater, perhaps, than fell to any English musician since ... Purcell – were so very seldom employed in work worthy of them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[274]</a></sup> Edward Elgar, to whom Sullivan had been particularly kind,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[275]</a></sup> rose to Sullivan's defence, branding Fuller Maitland's obituary "the shady side of musical criticism ... that foul unforgettable episode."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[n 27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fuller Maitland's followers, including <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Walker_(composer)" title="Ernest Walker (composer)">Ernest Walker</a>, also dismissed Sullivan as "merely the idle singer of an empty evening".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes.2C_p._6_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes.2C_p._6-306">[279]</a></sup> As late as 1966, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Howes" title="Frank Howes">Frank Howes</a>, music critic of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> condemned Sullivan for a "lack of sustained effort ... a fundamental lack of seriousness towards his art [and] inability to perceive the smugness, the sentimentality and banality of the Mendelssohnian detritus ... to remain content with the flattest and most obvious rhythms, this yielding to a fatal facility, that excludes Sullivan from the ranks of the good composers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[280]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:D%27oyly-carte-the-joy-of-three-generations-1921.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="347" data-file-width="299" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4d/D%27oyly-carte-the-joy-of-three-generations-1921.jpg/220px-D%27oyly-carte-the-joy-of-three-generations-1921.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4d/D%27oyly-carte-the-joy-of-three-generations-1921.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:D%27oyly-carte-the-joy-of-three-generations-1921.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
20th century audiences</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Frederick_Dunhill" title="Thomas Frederick Dunhill">Thomas F. Dunhill</a> wrote in 1928 that Sullivan's "music has suffered in an extraordinary degree from the vigorous attacks which have been made upon it in professional circles. These attacks have succeeded in surrounding the composer with a kind of barricade of prejudice which must be swept away before justice can be done to his genius."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[281]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wood" title="Henry Wood">Sir Henry Wood</a> continued to perform Sullivan's serious music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[282]</a></sup> In 1942, Wood presented a Sullivan centenary concert at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Albert_Hall" title="Royal Albert Hall">Royal Albert Hall</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grove_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-grove-31">[29]</a></sup> but it was not until the 1960s that Sullivan's music other than the Savoy operas began to be widely revived and reassessed. In 1960 Hughes published the first full-length book about Sullivan's music "which, while taking note of his weaknesses (which are many) and not hesitating to castigate his lapses from good taste (which were comparatively rare) [attempted] to view them in perspective against the wider background of his sound musicianship."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes.2C_p._6_306-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes.2C_p._6-306">[279]</a></sup> The work of the Sir Arthur Sullivan Society, founded in 1977, books about Sullivan by musicians such as Young (1971) and Jacobs (1986), and a growing number of recordings have contributed to the re-evaluation of Sullivan's serious music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burton_303-1"><a href="#cite_note-burton-303">[277]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Symphony in E</i> had its first professional recording in 1968, and a considerable number of Sullivan's non-Gilbert works have since been recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sulldiscog_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sulldiscog-310">[283]</a></sup> Scholarly critical editions of a growing number of Sullivan's works have been published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grove_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-grove-31">[29]</a></sup> In a 2000 article in <i>The Musical Times</i>, Nigel Burton wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[Sullivan] spoke naturally to all people, for all time, of the passions, sorrows and joys which are forever rooted in the human consciousness. He believed ... that the human being's prime duty in life is to serve humanity. It is his artistic consistency in this respect which obliges us to pronounce him our greatest Victorian composer. Time has now sufficiently dispersed the mists of criticism for us to be able to see the truth, to enjoy all his music, and to rejoice in the rich diversity of its panoply. ... [L]et us resolve to set aside the "One-and-a-half-hurrahs" syndrome once and for all, and, in its place, raise THREE LOUD CHEERS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burton_303-2"><a href="#cite_note-burton-303">[277]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recordings">Recordings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Recordings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On 14 August 1888, <a href="/wiki/George_Edward_Gouraud" title="George Edward Gouraud">George Gouraud</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a>'s phonograph to London in a press conference, including the playing of a piano and cornet recording of Sullivan's "The Lost Chord", one of the first recordings of music ever made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edison_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edison-311">[284]</a></sup> Sullivan was invited to a party on 5 October 1888 given to demonstrate the technology. After dinner, he recorded a speech to be sent to Edison, saying, in part:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I can only say that I am astonished and somewhat terrified at the result of this evening's experiments: astonished at the wonderful power you have developed, and terrified at the thought that so much hideous and bad music may be put on record forever. But all the same I think it is the most wonderful thing that I have ever experienced, and I congratulate you with all my heart on this wonderful discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edison_311-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edison-311">[284]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>These recordings were discovered in the Edison Library in New Jersey in the 1950s:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edison_311-2"><a href="#cite_note-Edison-311">[284]</a></sup></p>
<div class="medialist listenlist noprint">
<ul>
<li style="list-style: none; display: inline">
<div class="haudio">

</div>
<div class="haudio">
<div class="fn" style="padding:2px 0;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Sullivan_-_wax_cylinder_recording.ogg" title="File:Arthur Sullivan - wax cylinder recording.ogg">After-dinner speech at the Little Menlo</a></div>

</li>

</ul>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mikado-1917.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1566" data-file-width="1000" height="345" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/33/Mikado-1917.jpg/220px-Mikado-1917.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/33/Mikado-1917.jpg/330px-Mikado-1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/33/Mikado-1917.jpg/440px-Mikado-1917.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mikado-1917.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Advertisement for the first recording of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mikado" title="The Mikado">The Mikado</a></i>, 1917</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first commercial recordings of Sullivan's music, beginning in 1898, were of individual numbers from the Savoy operas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[n 28]</a></sup> In 1917, the <a href="/wiki/Gramophone_Company" title="Gramophone Company">Gramophone Company</a> (also known as <a href="/wiki/HMV" title="HMV">HMV</a>) produced the first album of a complete musical score of a Gilbert and Sullivan opera, <i>The Mikado</i>, followed by recordings of eight more of the operas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[286]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sound_recording_and_reproduction#Electrical_recording" title="Sound recording and reproduction">Electrical recordings</a> of the complete musical scores of most of the operas were then issued by the Gramophone Company and <a href="/wiki/Victor_Talking_Machine_Company" title="Victor Talking Machine Company">Victor Talking Machine Company</a> beginning in the late 1920s. These recordings were supervised by <a href="/wiki/Rupert_D%27Oyly_Carte" title="Rupert D'Oyly Carte">Rupert D'Oyly Carte</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[287]</a></sup> The D'Oyly Carte Opera Company continued to produce recordings until 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[288]</a></sup> Between 1988 and 2003, after the company was revived, it recorded seven of the operas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Digital_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-Digital-317">[289]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other recordings have been made by opera companies such as <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Sullivan_for_All" title="Gilbert and Sullivan for All">Gilbert and Sullivan for All</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[290]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Opera_Australia" title="Opera Australia">Australian Opera</a>, among many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Digital_317-1"><a href="#cite_note-Digital-317">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[291]</a></sup> Ad hoc companies of operatic singers conducted by <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Sargent" title="Malcolm Sargent">Sir Malcolm Sargent</a> in the 1950s and 60s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[292]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mackerras" title="Charles Mackerras">Sir Charles Mackerras</a> in the 1990s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Digital_317-2"><a href="#cite_note-Digital-317">[289]</a></sup> have made audio sets of several Savoy operas, and in the 1980s <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Faris" title="Alexander Faris">Alexander Faris</a> conducted video recordings of most of the operas with casts including show-business stars as well as professional singers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[293]</a></sup> The long-running <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a> production of <i>The Pirates of Penzance</i> presented by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Papp" title="Joseph Papp">Joseph Papp</a>, re-orchestrated with synthesisers replacing the strings, was put on record in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[294]</a></sup> Since 1994, the <a href="/wiki/International_Gilbert_and_Sullivan_Festival" title="International Gilbert and Sullivan Festival">International Gilbert and Sullivan Festival</a> has released numerous professional and amateur CDs and videos of its productions and other Sullivan recordings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[295]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sullivan's non-Savoy works were infrequently recorded until the 1960s. A few of his songs were put on disc in the early years of the 20th century, including versions of "The Lost Chord" by <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Caruso" title="Enrico Caruso">Enrico Caruso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clara_Butt" title="Clara Butt">Clara Butt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[296]</a></sup> The first of many recordings of the <i>Overture di Ballo</i> was made in the 1940s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[297]</a></sup> Sullivan's <i>Irish Symphony</i> was first recorded in 1968 under <a href="/wiki/Charles_Groves" title="Charles Groves">Sir Charles Groves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[298]</a></sup> Since then, much of Sullivan's serious music and his operas without Gilbert have been recorded. Some highlights include<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sulldiscog_310-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sulldiscog-310">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[299]</a></sup> the cello concerto by <a href="/wiki/Julian_Lloyd_Webber" title="Julian Lloyd Webber">Julian Lloyd Webber</a> (1986),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[300]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Golden_Legend" title="The Golden Legend">The Golden Legend</a></i> under <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Corp" title="Ronald Corp">Ronald Corp</a> (2001)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[301]</a></sup> and <i>Ivanhoe</i>, under the conductor <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd-Jones_(conductor)" title="David Lloyd-Jones (conductor)">David Lloyd-Jones</a> (2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[302]</a></sup> Mackerras's Sullivan ballet score, <i><a href="/wiki/Pineapple_Poll" title="Pineapple Poll">Pineapple Poll</a></i>, has received many recordings since its premiere in 1951, four of them conducted by Mackerras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[303]</a></sup></p>
