<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_and_references">Notes and references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Sullivan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes and references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dt>Notes</dt>
</dl>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 24em; -webkit-column-width: 24em; column-width: 24em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Jenny_Lind" title="Jenny Lind">Jenny Lind</a>, devastated by the premature death of <a href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn" title="Felix Mendelssohn">Felix Mendelssohn</a> in 1847, waited a year to sing the soprano part in his oratorio <i><a href="/wiki/Elijah_(oratorio)" title="Elijah (oratorio)">Elijah</a></i>, which he had written for her. She finally did so at a concert in 1848, raising £1,000 to fund a scholarship in his name. After Sullivan became the first recipient of the scholarship, Lind encouraged him in his career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A few sources stated that Sullivan had some Jewish ancestry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> but Jacobs concludes that there is no evidence of any Jewish antecedents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Between 1861 and 1872 Sullivan worked as an organist at two fashionable London churches: St. Michael's, <a href="/wiki/Chester_Square" title="Chester Square">Chester Square</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pimlico" title="Pimlico">Pimlico</a> and St. Peter's, Cranley Gardens, <a href="/wiki/Kensington" title="Kensington">Kensington</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grove_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-grove-31">[29]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Grove described the discovery thus: "I found, at the bottom of the cupboard and in its farthest corner, a bundle of music books two feet high, carefully tied round, and black with the undisturbed dust of nearly half-a-century. ... There were the part books of the whole of the music in <i><a href="/wiki/Rosamunde" title="Rosamunde">Rosamunde</a></i>, tied up after the second performance, in December 1823, and probably never disturbed since. Dr. Schneider [curator] must have been amused at our excitement ... at any rate, he kindly overlooked it, and gave us permission to ... copy what we wanted."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The work received an enthusiastic public reception, but <i><a href="/wiki/The_Musical_Times" title="The Musical Times">The Musical Times</a></i> printed an early example of critical censure of Sullivan for his accessibility: "The applause which it received was general and spontaneous [but] it may be a question whether, if Mr. Sullivan could not be requested to furnish a higher class of work, he should not have been passed over altogether until a more fitting opportunity presented itself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[45]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This was for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Merchant_of_Venice" title="The Merchant of Venice">The Merchant of Venice</a></i> at the Prince's Theatre, Manchester.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[48]</a></sup> Sullivan's earlier <i>Tempest</i> music had been composed for the concert hall, rather than theatrical performance, although it was later used for at least one stage production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">With a classical story and a mixture of political satire and <a href="/wiki/Grand_opera" title="Grand opera">grand opera</a> parody, <i>Thespis</i> was reminiscent of <i><a href="/wiki/Orpheus_in_the_Underworld" title="Orpheus in the Underworld">Orpheus in the Underworld</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/La_belle_H%C3%A9l%C3%A8ne" title="La belle Hélène">La belle Hélène</a></i> by Offenbach, whose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operettas" title="Operettas">operettas</a> were extremely popular on the English stage in both French and English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[53]</a></sup> <i>La belle Hélène</i> entered the Gaiety's repertory eight weeks before the premiere of <i>Thespis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[54]</a></sup> Sullivan may have been encouraged to write the music for <i>Thespis</i> by Hollingshead's offer of the role of Apollo to the composer's elder brother, the comic actor and singer <a href="/wiki/Fred_Sullivan" title="Fred Sullivan">Fred Sullivan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[55]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The musical score of <i>Thespis</i> was never published and is now lost, except for one song that was published separately, a chorus that was re-used in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pirates_of_Penzance" title="The Pirates of Penzance">The Pirates of Penzance</a></i>, and the Act II ballet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[57]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sullivan composed 72 hymns, including two settings of "<a href="/wiki/Nearer,_My_God,_to_Thee" title="Nearer, My God, to Thee">Nearer, My God, to Thee</a>", of which the "Propior Deo" is the better known. Young gives a complete list of Sullivan's hymns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[63]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Carte conducted <i>Cox and Box</i> and some operettas on a tour, in 1871, managed by the composer's brother, Fred, who played Cox. <i>Cox and Box</i>, again with Fred as Cox, had been revived in 1874, and Arthur Sullivan may have been considering a return to comic opera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[66]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The title page of the libretto describes <i>Trial</i> as "A Dramatic Cantata";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[68]</a></sup> Gilbert and Sullivan insisted on calling the rest of their joint works "operas", often with a descriptive adjective, such as a "nautical comic opera" (Jacobs, p. 118), an "aesthetic opera" or a "Japanese opera" (Jacobs, Preface).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In a study of the School and its successor, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Music" title="Royal College of Music">Royal College of Music</a>, David Wright comments on Sullivan: "He lacked any fresh perspective on musical training and any vision of what the NTSM needed to achieve if it was to make a mark. ... Neither did Sullivan have real sympathy with the Society of Arts' progressive social ideals of scholarship education regardless of social origin, despite having himself gained his education through scholarship support."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-wright-90">[79]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gilbert, Sullivan and Carte tried for many years to control the American performance copyrights over their operas, but they were unable to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[88]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-112"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-112">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Viennese music critic <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Hanslick" title="Eduard Hanslick">Eduard Hanslick</a> wrote of Sullivan's conducting of a Mozart symphony: "Sullivan presides on the podium from the comfortable recesses of a commodious armchair, his left arm lazily extended on the arm-rest, his right giving the beat in a mechanical way, his eyes fastened on the score. ... Sullivan never looked up from the notes; it was as though he was reading at sight. The heavenly piece plodded along for better or for worse, listlessly, insensibly."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">Bernard Shaw</a>, who praised Sullivan as a composer ("They trained him to make Europe yawn, and he took advantage of their teaching to make London and New York laugh and whistle.") commented: "Under his <i>bâton</i> orchestras are never deficient in refinement. Coarseness, exaggeration, and carelessness are unacquainted with him. So, unfortunately, are vigor and earnestness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[98]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-115"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-115">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gilbert replied, "it most certainly never occurred to me to look for any other reward than the honour of being associated, however remotely and unworthily, in a success which, I suppose, will endure until music itself shall die. Pray believe that of the many substantial advantages that have resulted to me from our association, this last is, and always will be, the most highly prized."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[100]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Even after Gilbert made changes (but retained a magic lozenge that changed people into what they pretended to be), Sullivan could not accept it.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The longest-running piece of musical theatre was <a href="/wiki/Robert_Planquette" title="Robert Planquette">Robert Planquette</a>'s 1877 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Op%C3%A9ra-comique" title="Opéra-comique">opéra-comique</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Les_Cloches_de_Corneville" title="Les Cloches de Corneville">Les Cloches de Corneville</a></i>, which held the record until <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Cellier" title="Alfred Cellier">Alfred Cellier</a>'s operetta <i><a href="/wiki/Dorothy_(opera)" title="Dorothy (opera)">Dorothy</a></i> ran for 931 performances beginning in 1886.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[113]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<i>King Olaf</i>, <i>Caractacus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dream_of_Gerontius" title="The Dream of Gerontius">Gerontius</a></i> owe much to <i>The Golden Legend</i> – as, via them, does Walton's <i><a href="/wiki/Belshazzar%27s_Feast_(Walton)" title="Belshazzar's Feast (Walton)">Belshazzar's Feast</a></i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hulme_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-hulme-133">[116]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gilbert wrote, "We have a name, jointly, for humorous work, tempered with occasional glimpses of earnest drama. I think we should do unwisely if we left, altogether, the path which we have trodden together so long and so successfully. I can quite understand your desire to write a big work, well, why not write one? But why abandon the Savoy business? Cannot the two things be done concurrently? If you can write an oratorio like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Martyr_of_Antioch" title="The Martyr of Antioch">The Martyr of Antioch</a></i> while you are occupied by pieces like <i><a href="/wiki/Patience_(opera)" title="Patience (opera)">Patience</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Iolanthe" title="Iolanthe">Iolanthe</a></i>, can't you write a grand opera without giving up pieces like <i>The Yeomen of the Guard</i>?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WriteTwoOperas_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-WriteTwoOperas-141">[123]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sullivan continued, "I have lost the necessary nerve for it, and it is not too much to say that it is distasteful to me. The types used over and over again (unavoidable in such a company as ours), the <a href="/wiki/George_Grossmith" title="George Grossmith">Grossmith</a> part, the middle-aged woman with fading charms, cannot again be clothed in music by me. Nor can I again write to any wildly improbable plot in which there is not some human interest. ... You say that in serious opera, <i>you</i> must more or less sacrifice yourself. I say that this is just what I have been doing in all our joint pieces, and, what is more, must continue to do in comic opera to make it successful. Business and syllabic setting assume an importance which, however much they fetter me, cannot be overlooked. I am bound, in the interests of the piece, to give way. Hence the reason of my wishing to do a work where the music is to be the first consideration – where words are to suggest music, not govern it, and where music will intensify and emphasize the emotional effects of the words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[124]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-161">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sullivan's biographers and scholars of his work have censured Tennyson's text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j335_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-j335-157">[137]</a></sup> Gervase Hughes called it "puerile rubbish".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes.2C_p._24_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes.2C_p._24-146">[126]</a></sup> Percy Young found it "Devoid of any kind of merit whatsoever."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-y194_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-y194-158">[138]</a></sup> Sullivan's music was initially well-received,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Notes_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Notes-159">[139]</a></sup> but Sullivan's biographers were not impressed: "One of Sullivan's lamest ... resourceless in magic" (Young);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-y194_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-y194-158">[138]</a></sup> "[not] even one memorable number" (Jacobs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j335_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-j335-157">[137]</a></sup> Recent critics, however, have praised Sullivan's contribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Notes_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-Notes-159">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[140]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-189"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-189">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Caryl_Brahms" title="Caryl Brahms">Caryl Brahms</a> speculates that Sullivan's relationship with his friend the <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Duke_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Alfred, Duke of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Duke of Edinburgh</a> (who was married) included sex, but the only evidence cited is unspecified "Victorian cartoonists".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[167]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">One such flirtation was with "Anna", whom he met in Paris in 1878.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[177]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-214"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-214">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1871, Fred appeared as Cox in his brother's <i><a href="/wiki/Cox_and_Box" title="Cox and Box">Cox and Box</a></i> at the <a href="/wiki/Alhambra_Theatre" title="Alhambra Theatre">Alhambra Theatre</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[187]</a></sup> and he toured as Cox in his own production that summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[188]</a></sup> Later that year, he played Apollo in Gilbert and Sullivan's <i>Thespis</i> at the Gaiety Theatre, remaining at the Gaiety thereafter. He took his own company on tour in the summer of 1874, appearing in his brother's <i>Cox and Box</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Contrabandista" title="The Contrabandista">The Contrabandista</a></i>, and later that year he again played Cox, this time at the Gaiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[189]</a></sup> The next year, he created the role of the Learned Judge in Gilbert and Sullivan's <i><a href="/wiki/Trial_by_Jury" title="Trial by Jury">Trial by Jury</a></i>, a role that he would play in London and on tour for the rest of his career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[190]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-255"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-255">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sullivan could also play the oboe and bassoon, but less proficiently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young4_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-young4-6">[6]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-299"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-299">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gian Andrea Mazzucato wrote this glowing summary of Sullivan's career in <i>The Musical Standard</i> of 16 December 1899: [T]he English history of the 19th century could not record the name of a man whose "life work" is more worthy of honour, study and admiration than the name of Sir Arthur Sullivan ... it is a debatable point whether the universal history of music can point to any musical personality since the days of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haydn" title="Haydn">Haydn</a>, Mozart and Beethoven, whose influence is likely to be more lasting than the influence the great Englishman is slowly, but surely, exerting. ... I make no doubt that when, in proper course of time, Sir Arthur Sullivan's life and works have become known on the continent, he will, by unanimous consent, be classed among the epoch-making composers, the select few whose genius and strength of will empowered them to find and found a national school of music, that is, to endow their countrymen with the undefinable, yet positive means of evoking in a man's soul, by the magic of sound, those delicate nuances of feeling which are characteristic of the emotional power of each different race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[273]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-305"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-305">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fuller Maitland was later discredited when it was shown that he had invented a banal lyric, passing it off as genuine and condemning Sullivan for supposedly setting such inanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burton_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-burton-303">[277]</a></sup> In 1929 Fuller Maitland admitted that he had been wrong in earlier years to dismiss Sullivan's comic operas as "ephemeral".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[278]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-313"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-313">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first was "Take a pair of sparkling eyes", from <i>The Gondoliers</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[285]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
